==PAGE:5==
Abstract
In the machine learning community, the focus has long been to develop algorithms and
models with high predictive capabilities. Complex models such as deep neural networks
and advanced ensembles achieve state­of­the­art predictive performance. However, de­
ploying these models in high stake situations remains a challenge. Because of their com­
plexity, they are challenging to interpret and thus trust.
In this works, we consider a case the Data and Analytics Team at Ørsted recently faced.
The team wanted to infer France’s, Germany’s, and United Kingdoms’ past renewable
generation relative to the available capacity for 2000­2015 using metrological data. With
generation data from 2015­2019, they built a machine learning model that learned the re­
lationship between metrological data and generation. With the trained model, they could
infer the relative generation from 2000­2015. However, the teams had developed a ma­
chine learning model that Analysts and Traders (the stakeholders) did not understand.
Thus the solution was not trusted and henced not used.
This work will solve the same case. However we will solve it in the light of interpretability
machine learning literature. We analyzes the data behind the case and identifies underly­
ing misalignment with data quality compared to governmental statistics particular for the
United Kingdom and Germany’s offshore generation. We propose an algorithm to correct
this misalignment with data quality. We use methods from the literature on interpretable
machine learning to make models more interpretable and quantify how interpretable the
models relative to each other. We analyze the stakeholders and identify five underlying
problem­specific interpretability constraints for a model to be perceived as interpretable
by the stakeholders at Ørsted.
We create 18 models to solve the case problem using the literature on interpretable ma­
chine learning. The first models are highly interpretable and satisfy the five interpretability
constraints. The others models have gradually increased complexity and violate more of
the five interpretability constraints. This way, we show what we gain in terms of predictive
accuracy but lose in terms of interpretability. Ultimately the model with the best trade­
off has to be decided by the stakeholders at Ørsted. We recommend that the Data and
Analytics Teams consider a sparse generalized additive model. The model has similar
predictive performance (if not a little better) to the complex model the extreme gradient
boosting ensembles (XGBoost) with the same features.
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders

==PAGE:11==
1
Introduction
With growing, complexity and sophistication Artificial Intelligence (AI) can solve compli­
cated real­world problems. For these reasons, enterprises increasingly apply AI in busi­
ness contexts with the hopes it can boost productivity and innovation. With the widespread
use of AI by enterprises, the stakeholders are increasingly wondering what models are
doing. How can we harness the potential of AI but also assess the risk? If we are to go
beyond these considerations, we need to build trust in the models for the stakeholders.
To form trust, we have to form understanding. That drives some intriguing research ques­
tions: How can we explain AI? How do we make it interpretable to different stakeholders?
Moreover, what does interpretability mean?
There have been AI winters over the past 30 years because AI was unable to cope with the
expectations of the technology. However, with technology now fulfilling the promise, the
question may be whether we face another winter AI due to researchers focusing on build­
ing more powerful tools without thinking about winning the trust in the already developed
techniques.
1.1
Context and Motivation
In this work, we consider the Machine Learning (ML) branch of AI. In this branch we will
focus on how we can make ML interpretable such that the stakeholders will have an easier
time adopting the models. We will do this in context of a case Ørsted’s Data and Analytics
Team (DAT) at Ørsted recently faced. The DAT supports energy Traders and Analysts
with models and tools to aid the forecasting of the electricity generation and demand and,
thus, the electricity price. The energy traders at Ørsted generate a bulk of the company’s
profits by making decisions based on electricity price forecasts from DAT with potentially
millions of DKK at stake. Therefore we will focus on how we can create interpretable ML
model as decision support for Analysts and Traders.
ML has become a valuable tool for Ørsted. The technology has improved the forecasts
that are becoming increasingly important with an increasing stochastic energy generation
from renewable energy sources. More complex models can detect more complex patterns
in the market and improve the forecasted energy price. However, as the complexity of
the ML model advances, it becomes increasingly difficult to understand why the model
arrives at its prediction. In interpretable ML literature, this is a well­known phenomenon:
models with high accuracy tend to be difficult to understand/less interpretable. The DAT
experienced this phenomenon in practice as well. They have developed complicated
models that provided great forecasts, but stakeholders never used the models’ predictions
because the models were not understood and thus not trusted.
In recent year, the works on ML interpretability among researchers has gotten much at­
tention. The proposed methodologies for improving interpretability are still in their early
stages, making the field challenging to navigate. In this work, we apply some of the pro­
posed methods to create ML models with interpretability in mind and utilize methods that
help us understand the working of the trained model. We will do this for a recent case
DAT faced where they faced similar issues. The Analysts and Traders did not adopt their
proposed model, because it was not interpretable. We have described the case in the
upcoming section.
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
1
==PAGE:12==
1.2
The Ørsted Advanced Analytics Group’s Case
In a recent case, the DAT wanted to estimate a country’s past solar and wind renewable
electric generation based on historical meteorological data. They wanted to know what the
generation was from 2000 to 2019. The task is a bit backward from traditional forecasting
cases the team faces. Here DAT wanted to infer the past, not the future. However, the
DAT was interested in the past as scenario analyses for the future.
However, as the capacity of renewables is fundamentally different now than from the
start of the millennium, we are interested in inferring the relative generation. That we will
denote the load factor, it is the ratio between generation and the total capacity. Hence
the load factor is always a number between [0, 1]. The assumption is that the load fac­
tor generalizes independently from the capacity and only depends on the meteorological
conditions.
For the case, we will consider the three countries: France (FR), Germany (GE), and
United Kingdom (UK) and the three types of renewables generation: Offshore wind, on­
shore wind, and solar. Data that describes the generation for these three countries and
renewables type is publically available by ENTSO­E Transparency Platform1. Data is
available with an hourly resolution, however, only for the period of 01/01/2015 and on­
wards. We will consider 01/01/2015 ­ 31/12/2019. To get the relative measure, i.e., to
calculate the load factor, Ørsted has purchased data that describes the monthly develop­
ment in capacity for three countries and three renewables types.
From an additional external vendor, Ørsted has bought meteorological data that describes
the wind and solar conditions with an hourly resolution for 01/01/2000 ­ 31/12/2019. The
vendor data consist of 54 nodes, i.e., strategically geographical location in Western Eu­
rope.
The idea is to learn the relationship between the meteorological data from the 54 nodes
and the load factor for the period 01/01/2015 ­ 31/12/2015 with ML. With the trained ML
model, we can infer the load factor for the period 01/01/2000 ­ 31/12/2014, where the
generation is unknown. The model should, however, be interpretable such the Analysts
and Traders will adopt it.
1.3
Structure of the Thesis
This thesis is composed of six chapters and appendices. We have just been through first
Chapter 1: The introduction to the thesis. We have described the remaining five chapters
in the list below.
1. Chapter 2: Literature Review on Interpretable Machine Learning. Provides a
literature review on the challenges and techniques for improving interpretability in
machine learning.
2. Chapter 3: Data, Problem & Stakeholders. Describes the available data and the
found issues with data quality. In the context of the presented data, we formulate
the case problem more formally in light of the stakeholders’ requirements for the
machine learning solution.
1ENTSO­E, The European Network of Transmission System Operators represents 42 electricity trans­
mission system operators from 35 countries across Europe. ENTSO­E was established and given legal
mandates by the European Union (EU)’s Third Legislative Package for the Internal Energy Market in 2009,
aiming for further liberalization of the gas and electricity markets in the EU. The platform is an open­source
collection of electricity generation, transportation, and consumption data for the European market. [ENT].
2
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:13==
3. Chapter 4: Modeling. In this section, we will describe the different models we will
propose to solve the case. We will present how the trained models perform in terms
of the mean squared estimate. We have described the underlying theory behind the
models in the supporting Appendix A.
4. Chapter 5: Post ­hoc Analysis. We will analyze how interpretable the models are
using the stakeholders’ requirements and the methods presented in chapter 2. We
will utilize the model­agnostic method SHAP and the underlying Shapley value, de­
scribed in Appendix A, to analyze the complex models.
5. Chapter 6: Conclusion and Future Work. This chapter provides the conclusion
and suggestions for future work.
We have used Python and its available libraries for all implementation.

2
Literature Review on Interpretable
Machine Learning
At Ørsted, the DAT have had a hard time deploying advanced ML models even though
they showed excellent performance. The most often reason was that the trained model
lacked interpretability. The stakeholders did not understand the modes, and thus they did
not trust it. But what does interpretability in the realm of ML mean? Likewise, we should
ask ourselves the question interpretable to whom?
In this chapter, we will review the existing literature on interpretable ML. The research
around interpretable ML is a young but active field. To limit ourselves, we will focus on the
research in the field that relate to DAT case as well cherry­pick specialized methods and
frameworks suited for DAT case. That means we will only consider literature describing
interpretability for supervised ML for regression.
We have split the chapter into six sections, which we briefly describe in the list below.
1. Interpretability Is Ill­defined, Unquantifiable, and Subjective. In the first sec­
tion 2.1 we will explore that even though much recent research has focused on
interpretable ML, it remains challenging to define and quantify. We intend for this
section to motivate the remaining chapter.
2. Defining Interpretable Machine Learning. In the second section 2.2 we will de­
clare the definitions/vocabulary that we will adhere to in this thesis. Clear definitions
are needed to discuss interpretability for the DAT case.
3. Measuring interpretability ­ The Predictive, Descriptive, Relevant (PDR) Frame­
work. In third section 2.3 we will present a framework that tries to quantifying inter­
pretability. The framework considers three metrics for rating interpretability methods:
predictive accuracy, descriptive accuracy, and relevancy to benchmark interpretabil­
ity.
4. Interpretable to Whom? In the fourth section, we will discuss what interpretability
means for different stakeholders of ML models. We will utilize two frameworks to
analyze the stakeholders specific for DAT case. The framework allows us to identify
rules and their underlying objectives for interpretability and what tasks we need to
solve to fulfill the objectives. The analysis of the stakeholders will help us focus on
what interpretability methods are the most important for the DAT case.
5. Taxonomy of Interpretability Methods. In the fifth section, we will present a set
of interpretability methods according to our taxonomy. We will relate the method to
the three metrics from the PDR framework and the interpretability task we want to
solve.
6. Summary. We will summarize the above sections in the sixth and final sections
before moving on to the next chapter.
Let us explore the issues with interpretability and motivate the remaining read.
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
5
==PAGE:16==
2.1
Interpretability Is Ill­defined, Unquantifiable, and
Subjective
Supervised machine learning has shown its ability to learn complex patterns from data,
enabling it to make remarkable predictive results on unseen data. However, it remains
challenging to deploy ML models especially complex black­box models. And even more
challenging in critical/high stake settings. One possible reason for this is that humans
cannot comprehend the internal workings of complex ML models and understand how
they arrived at a specific decision. Researchers have recently broadened attention to
combat the issue by developing methods that help interpret what the model has learned.
Though more and more researchers are interested in this subject, the term interpretable re­
mains ill­defined. Research papers provide diverse and sometimes non­overlapping mo­
tivations for interpretability. Furthermore, they use the terms transparency, comprehen­
sibility, and explainability interchangeably [Lip16]. It remains unclear how the proposed
interpretation methods are related and what common concept to use to evaluate them
[Pre+18]. In other words, it is not clear how to measure/quantify interpretability. Finally,
the suggested interpretability methods proposed in papers do not necessarily explicitly
describe their intended user [Sur+21]. We should consider interpretability concerning a
specific user or user group as different stakeholders have different motivations for inter­
pretability. They need different interpretability methods when they interact with a machine
learning model [Tom+18]. The interpretation is subjective to the stakeholder.
In the upcoming section 2.2 we will define the vocabulary we will adhere to regarding
interpretability.
2.2
Defining Interpretable Machine Learning
To clarify ”interpretability,” we will split the term into two: Global and local interpretability.
• Global interpretability: Describes the level to which we can understand a ML model
as a whole. A model with a high degree of global interpretability is simple enough for
stakeholders to be understood independently. Here we differentiate between global
intrinsic interpretability and global post­hoc interpretability
1. Global intrinsic interpretability: Describes the level to which stakeholders can
understand the internal workings and structures of the chosen ML model. To
achieve high intrinsic interpretability, we have to resort to simple models. The
interpretable machine learning literature often refers to simple models as white­
box models. In contrast, it refers to complicated models as black­box models.
For example, we consider a linear regression model’s internal workings and
structure with a few features to be more intrinsically interpretable than the in­
ternal workings of a random forest model with many trees.
2. Global post­hoc interpretability: Describes the level to which we can extract
knowledge about a fully trained model and illuminate the parameters or learned
representation intuitively to stakeholders. In contrast to global intrinsic inter­
pretability, we consider the model after we have trained it.
• Local interpretability: Describes the level to which a ML model can clarify the cause
of its prediction based on the corresponding feature input. Here we again differenti­
ate between local intrinsic interpretability and local post­hoc interpretability:
1. Local intrinsic interpretability: We achieve high local intrinsic interpretability by
design if we use models with high global intrinsic interpretability. With global
6
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:17==
intrinsic models, we can easily understand how they make their predictions. For
instance, it is easy to explain how a linear regression model with few features
made its prediction based on the feature input, whereas it is more challenging to
account for a prediction from a black­box model such as a deep neural network.
2. Local post­hoc interpretability: If a model has low global intrinsic interpretability,
it is harder to account for its prediction. To learn more about how/why a model
made its prediction, we must consider local post­hoc methods. These methods
let us learn about how the fully trained model values what features for particular
predictions.
In section 2.5 we will explore several methods for increasing global and local interpretabil­
ity from an intrinsic and post­hoc view concerning the stakeholders’ different needs. The
difference between global and local interpretability that we will adhere to in this thesis are
often referred to as transparency and explainability in the literature. Unless otherwise
specified, the term interpretability will refer to both global and local interpretability.
In the upcomming section section 2.3 we will explore how to measure both global and
local interpretability by introducing the predictive, descriptive, relevant (PDR) framework,
proposed by [Mur+19].
2.3
Measuring interpretability ­ The Predictive, Descriptive,
Relevant (PDR) Framework
The choice of interpretation method depends on the nature of the problem we as data
scientists seek to solve and the interpretation needs for the stakeholders utilizing the de­
veloped ML model. To help us evaluate the appropriate interpretability method, we will
introduce the PDR framework proposed by [Mur+19]. The framework considers three
types of metrics as a guide for selecting the appropriate interpretation method: 1. predic­
tive accuracy, 2. descriptive accuracy, and 3. relevancy.
[Mur+19] suggests to incorporate the process of interpretable ML in the broader data­
science life cycle. We have displayed broader data­science life cycle in Figure 2.1. In
this section, we will re­discover the data­science life cycle in light of the PDR Framework.
Figure 2.1 shows that we need to consider the relevancy in the first stage of the cycle:
Problem, Data & Stakeholder. We measure the predictive accuracy in the Modeling stage
and evaluate the descriptive accuracy in the post­hoc stage. It is the same cycle we will
adhere to in this thesis.
Problem, Data & 
Stakeholders
Modeling
Post-hoc 
analysis
Iterate
Predictive 
accuracy
Descriptive
accuracy
Relevancy
Figure 2.1: The data science life cycle. We have marked the part where interpretability
is important with black text. Similarly, we have marked with red text where we evaluate
the three metrics: 1. predictive accuracy, 2. descriptive accuracy, and 3. relevancy from
the PDR­framework. We have adapted the figure and its description from [Mur+19], with
minor modifications.
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
7
==PAGE:18==
In the upcoming subsection, we will present each stage in the cycle and the associated
metric. We will go through the cycle in an odd order. The order will, however, make sense
in terms of the presentation of three metrics. We present the order below:
1. We will start by considering the second stage in the cycle: Modeling and the pre­
dictive accuracy metric.
2. Afterwards we consider the third stage: Post­Hoc Analysis and the descriptive
accuracy metric.
3. When we will present the first stage: Problem, Data & Stakeholder and the rele­
vancy metric.
4. We will not devote a section to the final stage: Iterate. If we found sufficient answers
to the problem, the cycle stops. If not, we need to update something in the chain
and re­iterate.
2.3.1
Data Science Cycle: Modeling
Based on the problem, the data, and stakeholders, we must select what predictive model(s)
to use and construct it. It is in this step that we measure a trained model’s predictive accu­
racy on a test set. Predictive accuracy describes how well the model(s) have learned the
general underlying relationship between the response and the features. For supervised
regression problems, a standard measure is the mean squared error between the predic­
tion of the model and the actual response in the test set. Predictive accuracy has long
been the main focus of studies around supervised ML and remains an essential aspect in
interpretable ML. If a model learns a poor underlying relationship between the response
and the features, any information extracted from the model is unlikely to be accurate ­ in­
cluding the interpretability methods. Thus for problems that require interpretability, we still
need an appropriate amount of predictive accuracy. Evaluating the predictive accuracy is
a quantitive analysis.
2.3.2
Data Science Cycle: Post­Hoc Analysis
After we have fit the chosen model(s) with the training data and measure its predictive
accuracy, we can analyze its descriptive accuracy. We define descriptive accuracy as
the degree to which an interpretation method has objectively captured the relationship
between features and response learned by the ML model. Unlike predictive accuracy,
descriptive accuracy is generally very challenging to measure. One way to ”measure” an
improvement is the ability to present better what the model has learned. Simple models
tend to be easier to present than complicated models. Evaluating the descriptive accuracy
is a qualitative analysis. In section 2.5 we will cover a range of method to improve the
descriptive accuracy.
2.3.3
Data Science Cycle: Problem, Data, and Stakeholders
At the beginning of the cycle, we must define the problem we wish to understand using
data. It is also in this phase, we process the data, e.g., clean and visualize it. The choice
of interpretation method depends on the defined problem, the available data, and the
stakeholders. We base the metric relevancy on the defined problem, the available data,
and the stakeholders. A human audience judges the metric.
For a reliable interpretation, we should maximize both the predictive accuracy and de­
scriptive accuracy. That often leads to the following dilemma: a simple model yields a
high descriptive accuracy but often has lower predictive accuracy, particularly on complex
datasets. On the other hand, a complex model might yield higher predictive accuracy, but
8
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:19==
it harder to analyze how the model works ­ it has low descriptive accuracy. The most im­
portant of the two types of accuracies depend on the last metric, namely the relevancy of
the underlying problem. Depending on the relevancy, we might prefer one accuracy over
the other. For instance, in problems requiring a justification or explanation models’ output
(local interpretability), we care more about descriptive accuracy than predictive accuracy.
For example, in applying for a bank loan, we are interested in knowing why the bank de­
clined our application and what we can do about it rather than a model that predicts a loan
applicant’s credit score with high accuracy.
Without assurance ML models output can be trusted to solve the problem, the stakehold­
ers will not adopt the model [Pre+18]. The stakeholders play such an essential part in
terms of all aspects of interpretability that we have devoted the entire upcoming section 2.4
to describe the literature on stakeholders and interpretability.
2.4
Interpretable to Whom? An Analysis of the Stakeholders
of Machine Learning Systems
The field of interpretability machine learning with stakeholders in mind is both vague and
specific. Some researchers focus on stakeholders as a general audience, whereas others
clearly define the stakeholders in the light of the machine learning system. That could be
the case where a model is used to diagnose a specific type of cancer. Here the stake­
holders are the doctors, the patients, and the model developers.
To limit ourselves, we will identify the stakeholders and their interpretability needs in the
light of DAT case. In a way, we start the first part of the data cycle, presented in previous
section 2.3, by defining the stakeholders. However, we will do this in the light of a literature
review. To describe the stakeholder and their interpretability needs for the DAT case, we
will consult the two frameworks:
1. The simple framework Stakeholders in Explainable AI proposed by [Tom+18], and
2. the more rigorous framework Beyond Expertise and Roles: A Framework to
Characterize the Stakeholders of Interpretable Machine Learning and their
Needs proposed by [Sur+21].
The first framework is simple. It proposes categorizing stakeholders by their role to the
machine learning model, thus guiding the analysis of each stakeholder’s relevant belief
and goals for specifying suitable interpretability measures for that stakeholder. The sec­
ond framework is much more rigorous. [Sur+21] summarizes the most recent literature on
interpretability for different stakeholders. It argues that in many contexts, the role­based
interpretability framework is too shallow. Primarily for the two reasons:
1. A role­based framework implicitly conflates a person’s expertise with what they need
from the system, and it does not acknowledge the rich tacit knowledge and lived
experience a person may possess.
2. Individual user’s roles might change over time. However, even if people remain in
the same role(s), their interpretability needs may change through repeated exposure
to the models.
While the critical points of view for rule­based interpretability proposed by [Sur+21] are
valid and certainly apply to DAT case, we will use the more simple rule­based framework.
The simple frameworks do not require as deep an understanding of Ørsted as an organiza­
tion, the people working there, and their history as the rigorous framework would require.
However, we will not use the simple framework alone. The rigorous framework [Sur+21]
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
9
==PAGE:20==
provides a three­level typology of interpretability needs that works smoothly with the rule­
based framework. We will use the three­level typology as an extension to the rule­based
framework. It enables us to analyze the different stakeholders’ goals and how we can
achieve them.
2.4.1
Roles at Ørsted Related to the DAT Case
In the framework proposed by [Tom+18] they originally define six different general types
of stakeholders. For the DAT case, only three of the roles make sense to consider. We
have illustrated the three roles and interactions with one another in Figure 2.2. We will
adhere to the title Ørsted uses for different rules, but the in Figure 2.2 we have marked
the title that [Tom+18] uses for a role in parenthesis below.

Figure 2.2: The three different stakeholders that interact with a machine learning model

We will now describe each of the three roles. Bear in mind that the description is a simpli­
fication1 of a complex organization with changing roles.
Data Scientists (Creators)
We, the Data Scientist (identified as creators in the framework by [Tom+18]), are the ML
model builders. We are responsible for designing and delivering data science­fuelled
models, products, and tools to the trading floor at Ørsted. It is our job to turn data into a
competitive advantage. We, the Data Scientist, have a robust understanding of modeling
but not the same qualitative understanding of the energy market compared to the Analyst.
We make products for the Traders; however, the Analysts monitor these products. The
Analysts is thus the stakeholder with the highest importance to us.
Analysts (Operators)
The Analysts (identified as operators) in the framework by [Tom+18]) are the user of the
machine learning model. They are the ones that deliver the model results to the Traders.
In other words, they are the link from the Data Scientists to the Traders. They previously
designed simple models for Ørsted, but they are now the ones that interact with the fin­
ished ML models created by the Data Scientist. They ensure that all new ML models align
with their domain knowledge of the fundamentals such as weather and pricing data that
affect the energy market. Analysts possess quantitative skills in dealing with data and
qualitative skills in domain knowledge about the energy market.
1As criticized by the more vigours framework proposed by [Sur+21].
10
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:21==
Traders (Executors)
The Traders (identified as Executors in the framework by [Tom+18]) are the type of stake­
holders who make decisions informed by the ML models. The Traders receive the infor­
mation from the ML models through the Analysts. Based on the information they receive,
they decide how to act in the energy market. They decide to buy or sell energy accord­
ingly. They are ultimately responsible for their decisions, sometimes with millions of DKK
at stake. The traders have a financial background and possess a thorough qualitative
understanding of the energy market.
Common to both the Traders and the Analysts is that they know the energy market well
and can often, by experience (and intuition), evaluate whether the model results make
sense even though they might not fully understand the modeling. We have identified a list
of constraints the models should obey to fit the Analysts’ expectation/intuition. We have
identified the points in the list through interviews with our contacts at the DAT. This list of
constraints acts as a set of methods that increase the descriptive accuracy relevant to the
problem. It makes sense to present the list of interpretability constraints after we present
the data. Thus the list will be presented in the upcoming chapter 3. In the upcoming
subsection, we will utilize the more rigorous framework to identify the three stakeholders’
underlying goals, objectives, and tasks.
2.4.2
Goals, Objectives, and Task for Interpretability
With the simple framework [Tom+18], we can identify that different stakeholders have
different needs for interpretability. However, the simple framework fails to identify the un­
derlying reasons for the different interpretability needs. The rigorous framework [Sur+21]
compresses the different stakeholders needs into three­level typology consisting of overall
goals, objectives and tasks. [Sur+21] defines two overall long­term goals for interpretabil­
ity that all stakeholders share:
(G1) Understand the model
(G2) Build trust in the model.
While both of these goals are vague and difficult to define precisely, we do not mean
accomplishing them directly. Rather these goals function like substrates that inform and
influence the shorter­term­objectives. We meet the objectives by solving a set of tasks.
[Sur+21] defines a total of seven objectives. For the DAT case we will only consider four
out seven objectives, as O3, O5 and O7 from [Sur+21], does not apply to the case. We
have described the five objectives in the list below and to which stakeholder the objective
is particularly important2.
(O1) Debug or improve a model.. Verify how the ML model is working (correctly or other­
wise), i.e., to aid system testing, debugging, improving the robustness, or increasing
the accuracy of the ML model. At Ørsted, this objective is particularly important for
the Data Scientists.
(O2) Ensure compliance with standards or regulation (expectation and intuition).
This objective is centering around auditing or ensuring that the development of ML
models are compliant with a particular set of standards (whether they are legal, eth­
ical, safety, or other). At Ørsted the ML models for the DAT case are not subject to,
e.g., legal requirement, but rather the models have to be compliant with expectation­
s/intuition of the Analysts’ domain knowledge of the fundamentals such as weather
effect on the energy market. The Analysts want assurance that the system’s output
2Recall the objective might be important to multiple stakeholders. However, as we use a broad and
straightforward framework, we instead consider to whom the objective is particularly import for.
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
11
==PAGE:22==
can be trusted, uncover inconsistencies between the models logic and their intuition
and expectations3.
(O4) Justify or explain actions influenced by a model’s output. To increase the trust
in ML models, they need to provide dynamic or ad hoc explanations of their decisions
in ways that are intelligible to humans. The Traders are interested in this objective
whenever a model does not align with their understanding of the market.
(O6) Learn about a domain. Stakeholders can use interpretability methods to generate
new hypotheses or insights about a domain. This objective is particularly important
for the Data scientists.
The third and last level of the interpretability typology, proposed by [Sur+21], identifies
five tasks (we will use all of them) we need to solve to achieve the objectives. Tasks
do not map to objectives in a one­to­one fashion; instead, the same task can help to
accomplish several different objectives. We describe the five tasks in the list below and
what objectives they solve for the DAT case.
(T1) Assess reliability of a given prediction. Understanding the reliability enable stake­
holder to make a distinction between more and less accurate predictions. It is im­
portant for identifying and explaining an outlier and improving the model (O1) by
repeatedly assessing the reliability of individual prediction.
(T2) Detect mistaken, discriminatory, or arbitrary behavior. The second task is to
ensure that we do not make predictions arbitrarily. The task is necessary to ensure
compliance with expectation and intuition (O2) and build trust in explaining action
influenced by the model (O4). In other cases, detecting incorrect reasoning is a way
to guide model debugging and elucidate areas for improvement (O1).
(T3) Understand the extent of the information the model is using. Understanding the
details and extent of features emerged as important for explaining actions influenced
by the model (O4). Furthermore, to ensure compliance with expectation (O2), the
features need to be understood.
(T4) Understand the influence of different features on the model’s output. For stake­
holders interested in generating new insights about a domain (O6), understanding
how different factors influence the output is critical. Understanding the influence of
different features is vital for explaining actions influenced by the model (O4) and for
ensuring compliance (O2) (or in our case, ensuring that the model aligns with the
Analysts expectations).
(T5) Understand model strengths and limitations. Understanding areas of weakness
is useful for debugging and improving the model (O1).

green color is associated with the Trader. From Figure 2.3 we can see that the different

2.5
Taxonomy of Interpretability Methods
Interpretable ML can be categories in terms of multiple criteria. We differentiate between
global and local interpretability. Furthermore, we differentiate between the two types of
interpretability methods, namely intrinsic and post­hoc methods. Both interpretation meth­
ods aim to increase the descriptive accuracy, but only the intrinsic interpretation methods
affect predictive accuracy. We have summarized how the two interpretability methods
affect the predictive and descriptive accuracy in Figure 2.4.
Within each of the intrinsic and post­hoc branches, there are multiple methods we can
use. The methods form the taxonomy of interpretation methods we will adhere to in this
thesis. We have summarized the taxonomy in 2.5 we in each leaf make the distinction
between local and global interpretability. This section will explore the taxonomy and how
the different methods can help us solve the five tasks identified in the previous section 2.4,

yield higher descriptive accuracy. Post­hoc interpretability methods (described in detail in
subsection 2.5.2) involve using methods to extract information from a trained model. As
we have trained the model, post­hoc methods do not affect the predictive accuracy. We
have adopted the figure from [Mur+19].

2.5.1
Intrinsic interpretability
We now discuss the intrinsic interpretability methods that come into play in the modeling
stage of the data–science life cycle (Figure 2.1). It is at this stage that we, as Data sci­
entists build ML models from the data. If we decide to build models with high intrinsic
interpretability, we will, by design, get high descriptive accuracy both on a global and a lo­
cal level. We will in this subsection explore three different intrinsic interpretability methods
14
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:25==
1. sparsity, 2. modularity, and 3. feature engineering. Each method provides a different
way to increase the descriptive accuracy by guiding us towards constructing models that
yield higher descriptive accuracy. Intrinsic interpretability generally reduces the predictive
accuracy and increases descriptive accuracy, but at times, we can boost both accuracies,
e.g., by enforced sparsity.
Sparsity
With a few nonzero parameters, we can easier understand the weight/variables associ­
ated with the parameters. Few nonzero parameters allow us to examine if the weight/vari­
ables are meaningfully related to the outcome in question (T2). A similar analysis would
be much more difficult to conduct for a model with many nonzero parameters.
Sparse models allow us to troubleshoot and reason about counterfactuals (T4, T5). E.g.,
we can examine how a prediction would change if we changed the input. This is only
possible if the number of nonzero parameters is meager as we humans can only handle
7 ± 2 cognitive entities at the same time [Mil56].
Furthermore, if the underlying relationship between features and targets originates from
a sparse set of signals, we can also improve predictive accuracy by enforcing sparsity.
In cases, sparsity will improve not only the descriptive accuracy but also the predictive
accuracy if we find the correct subset of features. Many methods incorporate sparsity
based on penalty on the loss function such as LASSO or model selection criteria such as
Akaike information criterion (AIC) or Bayesian information criterion (BIC).
Modularity
The term ”modular” refers to interchangeable components (or modules) in a model. If we
can interpret the prediction­making progress of the machine learning model independently,
we say a ML model is modular. A wide range of models satisfies modularity to different
degrees. For instance, we can perfectly interpret a linear model on a modular level. Each
weight is associated with individual features. From the weights, we can learn how the
features individually affect the prediction. Each weight and feature acts as a module, and
when we add up all modules, we get the prediction5.
By inspecting the individual modules, we can find potential counterintuitive properties in
the model and correct them. In conclusion, modular ML models give us a more extensive
chance for finding arbitrary behavior in the model (T2) as well as assessing the reliability
of prediction (T1) but also an easier way to understand the influence of features (T4).
Feature Engineering
The features used as input to a model have great importance. It is not only the type of
model that provides helpful intrinsic interpretation. The relationship we hope our model
learns between the target and the features is easier to learn if we use informative fea­
tures. More informative features mean we can get higher predictive accuracy. Further­
more, when the features have more meaning to a particular audience, they become easier
to interpret (T3, T4). In many individual domains, expert knowledge can help construct
features. We shall later see that the Analyst wants us to use a particular feature transfor­
mation of wind speed that better reflects the power production of wind turbines.
2.5.2
Post­hoc Interpretability
Post­hoc interpretability methods come into play in the post­hoc analysis stage of the
data–science life cycle (Figure 2.1). Recall, when we conduct post hoc analysis, the
model’s predictive accuracy is fixed. At this stage, we analyze a trained model to provide
5The interpretation of a single weight is interlocked with all other weights. The interpretation of a single
weight is interpreted when the other input features remain at the same value, which is not the case with many
real applications. Often the features are correlated, and that messes with weight estimation.
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
15
==PAGE:26==
insights into the learned relationships. There are to types os post­hoc methods: model­
specific and model­agnostic methods.
Model­specific interpretation methods are limited to specific model classes. Some model
has specific model­agnostic methods that other do not. Whereas we can use model­
agnostic methods can on any ML model. We can apply these methods after the training
of the model.
Post­hoc methods require us to distinguish between the two categories: local and global
interpretability, although they have much in common. Global post­hoc interpretability ap­
proaches often yield information at the local level, but its no guarantee.
Model­specific
It is easy to confuse model­specific methods with intrinsic ML models because there is a bit
of overlap. To distinguish between the two, consider a linear regression model. With this
model, we can learn about the data/domain we trained the model on by considering the
weight associated with parameters. The weights of linear regression is a model­specific in­
terpretation method. However, we only get the intrinsic global interpretability when limiting
the number of features in the model. Thus, models we perceive as intrinsic interpretable
are only intrinsic interpretable because they have great post­hoc model­specific proper­
ties, and those properties only come into play if we ensure simplicity (i.e., low amount of
features).
Model­specific methods are not limited to simple models such as linear regression. Model­
specific post­hoc­methods also exist for models that are inherently black­boxes. For in­
stance, we can figure out which features are the most important/relevant for making pre­
dictions with a random forest model. We can do so by examining each tree structure in the
forest. We collect how on average, a feature increases, e.g., the impurity (if we consider
a classification problem) for each feature. The average overall of the trees in the forest is
the measure of feature importance. While this method provides an idea on a global level
of what features are important, we do not learn how the model makes specific predictions
(local interpretability).
Model­Agnostic
We can view model­agnostic methods as an additional ML model that learns how our
trained model works by considering its input and output. In other words, we separate
the descriptive accuracy from the trained machine learning to a set of methods that we
can apply to any ML model. Researchers have developed several model­agnostic inter­
pretability. While none of them are perfect, they can improve the descriptive accuracy
even for very complex black box ML models6.
There exist many types of model­agnostic interpretability methods. When we apply any
model­agnostic methods to a model, the goal is to improve the descriptive accuracy. In the
list below, we can read a short description of the most popular model­agnostic methods.
We have described the methods with inspiration from the works of [Mol19].
• Partial Dependence Plot (PDP): In the plot, the x­axis constitutes the value of a
specific feature, and the y­axis constitutes the predicted value. The methods help
us determine what happens to the model’s predictions as the different features are
adjusted. In other words, PDP can help us analyze and visualize the interaction
between the target response and a set of input features of interest. The PDP allows
6Model­agnostic methods are not limited to black­box models. They work just as well on white­box models.
Sometimes a combination of an intrinsic white­box model with post­hoc model agnostic interpretation is the
ideal interpretability solution.
16
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:27==
us to interpret the partial dependence as the expected target response as a function
of the input features of interest. While PDP is intuitive and easy to implement, it might
hide heterogenous effects because it only shows the average marginal effects. For
example, one feature might show a positive relationship with a prediction for half of
the data but a negative relationship for the other half. Furthermore, PDP assumes
that the input features are independent. In practice, we often violate this assumption.
The method is a global interpretability method that helps us understand the influence
of features (T4) as well assessing the reliability of prediction (T1).
• Individual Conditional Expectation (ICE): ICE displays one line per instance. In­
stead of plotting an average like PDP. The ICE shows the instance’s prediction
changes when a feature changes. Like partial dependence, ICE helps explain what
happens to the model’s predictions as a particular feature varies. With one line per
instance, the method is local, but it shows all cases; hence it is also a global method.
The method helps us understand the influence of features and the reliability of pre­
dictions (T4, T1).
• Global Surrogate: Global Surrogate models work differently than the methods men­
tioned above. Here we use what is perceived as an interpretable model to approxi­
mate the ML model of our interest. We train the surrogate model on the predictions
from the model of our interest. The trained interpretable model now becomes a sur­
rogate of the original model, and all we need to do is interpret the surrogate model.
Using an interpretable model to approximate the model, we try to learn more in­
troduces additional errors, but R­squared can easily measure the additional error.
However, it is unclear what the best cut­off for R­squared is to be confident that the
surrogate model is close enough to the black­box model. Is it 80 % of variance ex­
plained? 50 %? 99 %? Furthermore, we must beware that we conclude the model
and not the data since the surrogate model never sees the actual outcome. Global
Surrogate is a global post­hoc interpretability method.
• Local Surrogate: Local Interpretable Model­agnostic Explanations (LIME) :
LIME does not try to explain the whole model. Instead, it trains interpretable models
to approximate the individual predictions to explain these. LIME attempts to com­
prehend how the predictions of our model change when we perturb the data. The
learned model should be a good approximation of the machine learning model pre­
dictions locally and in the neighborhood around the prediction, but it does not have
to be an excellent global approximation.
With the R­squared measure, we can easily measure how good our surrogate mod­
els are in approximating the black box predictions. LIME share some of the same
issues as the Global Surrogate: is unclear how much variance we need to explain
and how large the neighborhood should be. The technique can help us assess the
reliability of a given prediction (T1) as well as help us understand the influence of
features (T4).
• SHapley Additive exPlanations (SHAP): SHAP is a method to explain individual
predictions. The Shapley value, from cooperative game theory, is the underlying
concept [LL17].
In the context of interpretable machine learning, we assume that the machine learn­
ing model is a ”game,” and each feature is a ”player,” and the prediction is the ”pay­
out” of the game. Each player’s contribution is measured by adding and removing
the player from all the players’ subsets. The Shapley Value for one player is the
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
17
==PAGE:28==
weighted sum of all its contributions across the subsets.
Shapley Value is additive and locally accurate. If we add up the Shapley Values of
all the features, plus the base value, which is the prediction average, we will get
the exact prediction value. That is a feature that other methods do not have. For
instance, the LIME is only a local approximation. The Shapley Value is only of the
mentioned methods to deliver a complete interpretation, and it is the explanation
method with the most robust theoretical basis. We can use SHAP to explain how
the model made a prediction (T2) as well as create SHAP Dependence Plot, which
shows each feature’s global effect the reliability of predictions (T1, T4).
This thesis will utilize SHAP to increase the descriptive accuracy of black­box mod­
els. We have described the theory behind SHAP in chapter 5.
2.6
Summary
In this chapter, we have reviewed the existing literature describing interpretable ML. We
learned the following:
• The field is new, and terminology still has not been settled. We thus clarified a
definition for interpretability we will adhere to in this thesis.
• We can use the PDR framework to benchmark interpretability. In the framework,
we differentiate between predictive accuracy and descriptive accuracy. Which of
the two is the most important is problem­dependent. We thus have to consider the
relevancy of the problem and stakeholders.
• Stakeholders play a vital part in terms of interpretability. They decide what is inter­
pretable.
• We can analyze stakeholder stakeholders by examining their rule and understand
their underlying objectives for their interpretability needs with an extension to a rule­
based framework.
• We can achieve the interpretability objectives by solving a set of tasks.
• There exists many different methods for increasing intrinsic interpretability and de­
scriptive accuracy in light of the identified task. We can use both intrinsic methods
and post hoc methods.
• Both post­hoc and intrinsic interpretability methods aim to increase descriptive ac­
curacy, but only intrinsic methods affect the predictive accuracy.
18
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:29==
3
Data, Problem & Stakeholders
In this chapter, we will step into the first part of the data­science life cycle presented in
Figure 2.1. This section will describe the underlying data, formalize the case problem,
and the Analysts’ interpretability requirements (stakeholders). The three together form
the relevancy1 metric for the case.
We have split the chapter into five sections.
1. Generation Capacity Data, and the Load Factor. Here we will explore the gener­
ation data extracted from the ENTSO­E Transparency Platform, the capacity data
provided by Ørsted, and the fraction between the two, namely the load factor. The
section will describe some issues/misalignments identified with the data quality and
how we elevated the issue.
2. Metrological Data. This section will explore the independent variables/features we
will use to predict the load factor. That is metrological data recorded at 54 different
locations in Western Europe.
3. Case Problem. After an introduction to the data, we will formulate the DAT case
more formally. Briefly described, we seek to develop a model that can predict the
historical load factor, i.e., the fraction generation over capacity.
4. Stakeholders: The Analysts’ Requirements to the Model. Before we end the
chapter, we will introduce the Analysts’ requirements for what they perceive as an
interpretable model.
5. Summary. We will finish the chapter with a summary of the previous sections.
We will spend the vast majority of the chapter in the first two sections devoted to exploring
the available data we will use to build our models.
3.1
Generation, Capacity Data, and the Load Factor
This section will explore the provided capacity data, the extracted generation, the identified
issues/misalignment, and how we elevated the issue. This section consists of a total of 7
subsections. We have provided an overview below:
1. Generation Data. This subsection will briefly describe the generation data and how
we extracted it.
2. Capacity Data. In the subsection, we will learn more about the capacity provided
by Ørsted.
3. Initial Load Factor. The subsection will describe the initial load factor based on
the provided capacity data and the extracted generation data. The initial load fac­
tor’s aggregated yearly mean value will be compared to governmental statistics and
illuminate potential issues.
4. Description of the Mean Yearly Load Factor Calculated From Governmental
Statistics. We will, in this subsection, learn more about the governmental statistics
1Recall in the PDR­Framework we consider three metrics: relevancy, predictive accuracy, and descriptive
accuracy. Relevancy is problem­dependent and used to evaluate which of the two types of accuracies are
the most important.
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
19
==PAGE:30==
data used to evaluate the initial load factor, describe where we found it, and how we
processed it.
5. Analysis of the Yearly Aggregated Generation. The subsection will explore how
the extracted generation data differs on aggregated yearly level from the govern­
mental statistics.
6. Analysis of the Provided Capacity Data. Likewise, we will examine how the ca­
pacity data provided by Ørsted differs from the governmental statistics.
7. Adjusting the Load Factor. Finally, we will end the section with a subsection de­
voted to proposing an algorithm that fits the capacity to the generation such that the
calculated load factor aligns better with the governmental statistics and the overall
expectation. The adjusted load factor is what we will model moving forward.
This section contains many figures describing the mentioned issues/misalignment. The
data we have based these figures on we can see in the form of tables in Appendix B.
References to the table will be made along the way as we proceed through the section.
Let us dive into the first subsection and learn more about the generation data.
3.1.1
Generation Data
We have extracted the generation data we will be working with from the open­source col­
lection of electricity transportation and consumption data or the European market known
as the ENTSO­E transparency platform. Data can be downloaded directly on the platform,
but we have more conveniently used the Python API. It allows us to extract the data in
tabular format in a customizable time range.
It was possible to extract full­time­series data for the periods 01/01/2015 ­ 31/12/2019
describing the hourly electricity generation for offshore wind, onshore wind, and solar
for each of the three countries; France, Germany, and the United Kingdom. We have
converted the time series have to UTC. Unfortunately, there was no data available for the
period before 2015. Table B.3 provides a summary of the extracted data. We can see for
the UK there are recorded negative observations. Furthermore, we can see there is not
an equal amount of recorded observation for the generation types. That is due to missing
observations (NaN’s). When we model the load factor, we will drop the metrological data
with a corresponding time index where we have NaN’s.
Table B.4 list the number of recorded negative observations per country and generation
types. As this naturally can not occur, we have replaced the negative observations with
NaN’s. We can see the number of NaN’s after doing this operation in Table B.4. To limit
the number of NaN’s the missing observations, we have linearly interpolated the missing
observations if there were no more than three consecutive missing observations in a row.
After the interpolatiton the number of missing observations can be seen in Table B.6.
3.1.2
Capacity Data
The DAT has provided us with the monthly development of renewable capacity for the
three energy types solar, offshore, and onshore wind for the three countries France, Ger­
many, and the United Kingdom. We can see the provided capacity data in Table B.2 for
the same period we have extracted generation data from (01/01/2015 ­ 31/12/2019).
The capacity data denotes the maximum amount of renewable energy a country can gen­
erate if, e.g., every single wind turbine performs at 100 % or every single solar panel
performs at 100 %. Naturally, we find the different renewable energy generating units
20
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:31==
spread throughout the country, and hence we can not expect them to have similar metro­
logical conditions, and thus it is unlikely that they all will be producing at a 100 % capacity.
Furthermore, individual units might need service, dragging the overall generation down.
In summary, it very unlikely we will experience the entire fleet of renewable energy gen­
erating units performing at their maximum capacity.
However, we can expect the generation to be closer to the capacity when the weather
condition is favorable for renewable energy generation. For instance, when it is very windy
in the entire country, we expect electricity generation from wind turbines to be closer to
the capacity than otherwise.
The provided capacity data has a monthly resolution ­ much more coarse than the granular
hourly resolution the generation data comes in. To calculate the load factor, we need
it on an hourly level. We have upscaled the data by linearly interpolating nonexisting
values between the monthly observations to combat the issue. The upscaled capacity is
visualized for the three countries for the three types in Figure 3.1 with dashed lines.
3.1.3
Initial Load Factor
The load factor is calculated as the ratio between the generation and capacity at specific
time index t i.e.
Load Factort = Generationt
Capacityt
,
∀t ∈{1, . . . n}.
(3.1)
Where n is the number of observations.
In Figure 3.1 are the provided capacity lin­
early upscaled and generation data visualized for the period of available generation data
(01/01/2015 ­ 31/12/2019).
At first eyesight, we might conclude that the ratio between the capacity and the generation,
i.e., the load factor, looks reasonable. The capacity data is consequently more consider­
able than the generation while it does not drift away from the generation data. However,
when we examine the yearly aggregated mean values for the different load factors, we
see a different picture. We can read the aggregated mean value in Table B.7. When we
examine Table B.7 particularly, the offshore load factors are lower than we initially expect
given our domain knowledge about wind energy generation.
Indeed the calculated load factors are off when we compare them to the official govern­
mental statistics on load factors presented in Table B.8. We have estimated the gov­
ernmental load factors from official statistics on yearly renewable energy generation and
development of capacity. In the upcoming subsection 3.1.4 we will explore how exactly
the governmental statistics were found and processed.
The difference between the calculated load factor, presented in Table B.7, and the govern­
mental statistics, Table B.8, is much more clear when we consult Figure 3.2. The figure
gives us a visual interpretation of the difference between the governmental statistics and
the calculated yearly aggregated mean load factors. The difference in load factor is par­
ticularly large for the offshore wind generation for Germany and the United Kingdom. For
the United Kingdom, there even appears to be a growing trend between the calculated
and official statistics. The differences are in a more acceptable region of −10% to +10%
for other renewable generations.
We will later explore how the governmental statistics differ from the calculated yearly mean
load factor in upcoming subsections. Essentially there are two reasons for the difference:

3.1.4
Description of the Mean Yearly Load Factor Calculated From
Governmental Statistics
This section will explore how the governmental load factors were created by consider­
ing external governmental statistics on renewable energy generation and corresponding
renewable capacity.
Sources that describe yearly mean load factor development on a yearly level are gener­
ally challenging to find. Instead, we will consult data that describes the yearly electricity
generation data and the development of the renewable capacity and estimate the yearly
load factor from these two numbers. It was possible to find data that describe the annual
generation and capacity for the period 2014­2019 (note we include 2014, a year before
we were able to extract generation data from the ENTSO­E Transparency Platform). The
list below describes the sources used for each of the three countries.
• Germany. The data is made publicly available by The Federal Ministry for Economic
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
23
==PAGE:34==
Affairs and Energy (Bundesministerium für Wirtschaft und Energie) [Bun19].
• United Kingdom. The data is made publicly available by The Department for Busi­
ness, and Industrial Strategy [US20].
• France. We tried to find an equivalent governmental source on the French Ministry
of Ecology Sustainable Development and Energy’s (Ministère de l’Écologie) web­
site but without luck. Instead we used British Petroleum’s (BP) Statistical Review of
World Energy ­ all data, 1965­2019[Bri20]. Note that this source does not differenti­
ate between onshore and offshore wind generation; however, before 2020, France
did not have any offshore wind generation, so the problem is elevated.
Though we could not find an official governmental source for France, we will still refer to
this data as ”governmental statistics.”
In Appendix C we elaborated further how the external govemental data was processed
and Table 3.1 summarizes in what section of Appendix C we can find greater details about
the extracted governmental data. Table 3.1 also show what tables contain the yearly
renewable energy generation and development and the corresponding capacity for each
of the three countries.

raw governmental data describing the development of yearly renewable energy genera­

The listed cumulative capacity and the aggregated generation from the tables’ references
above is an end of the year measure. It shows the aggregated generation cumulative
capacity at December 31’st.2 For instance, if we considered the offshore wind capacity
for Germany in 2015 was 3283 MW December 31’st (Table C.4). Likewise we see corre­
sponding generation was 8.28 TWh (Table C.3). It is important to note that the aggregated
generation is based on the continuous development of the offshore capacity. Hence we
have to consider the capacity at the start and end of the year. For the year 2015, we know
the capacity of the 31’st of December was 3283 MW. To get the measure for the start of
the year, we can use the observation from 31/12/2014. Here we see the capacity was
994 MW. That means throughout year 2015 the offshore capacity for Germany grew with
3283[MW] −994[MW] = 2289[MW].
The generation is a product of the capacity growth and not just the capacity listed at the
end of the year. As the governmental data does not contain information about which
month’s capacity increases, we need to make assumptions. We will assume that capacity
growth happened linearly throughout the year.
2Note we could verify whether the capacity is an end of the year measure or a start of the year measure.
However, the end of the year measure aligns best with the monthly provided capacity shown in Table B.2. This
is confirmed by examing Figure 3.5 here is the governmental capacity plotted as an end of the year measure
and compared to the first month of the year of the provided capacity. They more or less align (except for
the offshore wind generation in Germany, but we will explore that further.). Hence we will assume that the
cumulative capacity data is an end of the year measure.
24
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:35==
To formalize how we can estimate the yearly average load factor we need a few definitions,
let
1. Cy [MW]: Denote the capacity for year y,
2. Gy [TWh]: Denote the actual generation for year y, and
3. y ∈{2014, 2015, 2016, 2017, 2018, 2019} denote each of the years we have gover­
mental data from.
With the above defination we can calculate the yearly potential generation3 Py [TWh] with
Equation 3.2.
Py = 24 [hours] · 365 [days] · Cy [MW]
106
(3.2)
Finally we can estimate load factor LFy [%] with Equation 3.3:
LFy =
Gy [TWh]
1
2 (Py−1 [TWh] + Py [TWh])
if y ≥2015
(3.3)
Note that for the same period (2015­2019), we have hourly generation data from the
ENTSO­E transparency platform. We utilize the mean of year y’s potential generation
and the mean of potential generation the year before, i.e., y −1. We do this to incorporate
the assumptions that capacity grows linearly throughout the year. This explains why 2014
was included in raw data to approximate the load factor for 2015 and onward.
With a clear description of how we created Table B.8 using governmental statistics, we
can proceed to explore how it differs from the calculated mean yearly load factor. We will
start by examing how yearly aggregated generation differs for the governmental data and
the data from the ENTSO­E Transparency Platform for each of the renewable types and
each country.
3.1.5
Analysis of the Yearly Geneation Aggregated
This subsection will explore how the yearly aggregated generation based on the ENTSO­
E generation data differs from the governmental statistics. In Table B.9 we can see the
calculated yearly geneation. Similarly, we can see the governmental statistics on the
yearly generation summarized in table Table B.10.
For an easier comparison of the two tables, we can consult Figure 3.3. The figure visu­
alizes how the governmental statistics, in general, tend to be more optimistic regarding
the yearly aggregated generation. For France and Germany, the difference between the
calculated electric generation and governmental statistics is around 10 percent or less for
all five years for all three types of generation. It is not easy to reason why this difference
exists. For example, perhaps ENTSO­E publishes the data differently from how the gov­
ernment publishes data, i.e., differences in standards. As the relative difference between
governmental data and ENTSO­E for two countires France and Germany, is modest, we
will conclude that the ENTSO­E generation data is not the reason for error concerning
these two countries’ calculated load factors.
While the calculated aggregated generation for France’s and Germany’s renewable gen­
eration looks reasonable compared to the official governmental statistics, the relative dif­
ference for United Kingdom’s generation is much more significant. For all revenable types

load factor from the governmental statistics presented Table B.8 as well they match the

DAT’s assumptions. As we will see in the upcoming subsection 3.1.6 the provided ca­
pacity for the United Kingdom align with governmental statistics hence the misalignments
for calculated load factor must be because ENTSO­E Transparency Platform potentially
underestimates the generation. The potential under­estimate naturally affects the calcu­
lated load factor and explains why we estimate lower load factors than the governmental
statistics. However, as it is not the scope of this thesis to investigate this difference, we
will let it be. Nonetheless, if generation data from the ENTSO­E transparency platform for
the United Kingdom, particularly offshore wind data, are analyzed in the future, it will be
worth investigating this difference in greater detail. Specifically, offshore wind energy in
the United Kingdom is an important market for Ørsted.
To compensate for misalignment with load factors, we will propose an algorithm in subsec­
tion 3.1.7 that adjusts the initially calculated load factor. In the upcoming subsection 3.1.6
we will explore how the provided capacity data might negatively influence the calculated
load factor and how it differs from governmental statistics.
3.1.6
Analysis of the Provided Capacity Data
In this subsection, we will explore how the capacity data provided by Ørsted differ from
the governmental statistics. Recall we can see the provided capacity data in Table B.2
and it is visualized in Figure 3.1 with dashed lines.
The provided monthly capacity data has a monthly which is much more granular than the
governmental statistics that are published yearly. To compare the two data set, the first
month of the year in the provided is compared to the governmental statistics the year
before as the governmental statistics is an end­of­year measure. Thus the governmental
capacity for, e.g., 2014 includes the cumulative capacity installed throughout the year
2014. Hence the year before for the governmental data is comparable to the first month
of the year for provided capacity.
We can see the capacity for the first month of the year for the provided capacity data
Table B.12. Similarly, the governmental data is summarized in Table B.11 note in the
table we have adjusted the year, so it is comparable to the provided data. The difference
between the two is more evident if we examine the two figures Figure 3.4 and Figure 3.5.
In Figure 3.4 are all renewables capacities for all countries shown except the offshore
wind capacity for Germany. From the figure, we can see that the provided capacity is
very close to governmental statistics in almost all cases, with governmental statistics for
France’s solar capacity being more than 10 % above the provided. On the other hand, the
difference in capacity for offshore wind for the United Kingdom is almost zero. In general,
the differences between the provided capacity and governmental statistics presented in
Figure 3.4 seem to be in a reasonable range.
However, the difference in capacity for offshore wind in Germany is much larger. The
difference is so much more considerable it is the reason why it has been plotted separately
in Figure 3.5. From the figure, it is apparent that the provided capacity data does not align
with governmental statistics for German offshore wind ­ especially in the beginning of the
periods where it differs as much as 250 %. This explains the high difference in load factor
for German wind offshore we saw in Figure 3.2.
3.1.7
Adjusting the Load Factor
In this subsection, we will utlize an algorithm for adjusting the calculated load factor by
refitting the capacity to the generation data. In this section we conceuptually describe the
algorithm and why we deemed it necessary. The algorithm itself is described in greater
detail in Appendix D.

The initial calculated load factor, presented in Figure 3.2 does not align with either ex­
pectation or governmental statistics. In particular, the misalignment in yearly mean load
factor for the offshore wind load factor for Germany and United Kingdom was large. Both
of these two countries and types of renewable production have considerable importance
for Ørsted, emphasizing a greater need for adjusting the calculated load factor before we
proceed to any modeling.
The proposed algorithm, described in Appendix D, only refits the capacity and not the
generation; hence it will not solve the issues with generation data. However, the hope
is that the ENTSO­E data still resembles the nature of the electric generation, e.g., the
offshore wind generation is higher when the wind speeds are higher. Moreover, we will
still achieve load factor measures according to the governmental data and expectations
by adjusting the capacity. We need to emphasize this no perfect algorithm.

in Figure 3.6. In general, the refitted capacity algorithm ensures a better fit compared
to the governmental statistics than the initial aggregated mean load factor presented in

3.2
Metrological Data
This section will explore the metrological data provided by Ørsted. Ørsted bought the data
from an external vendor. The metrological data will act as our features in the models that
will predict the load factor.
The data has been recorded hourly in the period 01/01/2000 ­ 31/12/2019. However, we
will focus on the period 01/01/2015 ­ 31/12/2019 as this is the time range where we have
available generation data. At each node point, the vendor has recorded the following
metrological variables:
• Tempeature [◦C]: Denotes the temperature in degrees Celcius, at given node at a
given time.
• Wind speed [m/s]: Denotes the speed of the wind, measured in meters per second,
30
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:41==
at a given node at a given time.
• Wind direction [◦]: Denotes the direction of the wind, measured in degrees, at a
given node at a given time.
• Sun load adjustable [%]: The measure describes how well adjustable solar panels
would perform at a given time at that node location. The vendor has recorded the
measure between [0­100] %. However, we have normalized it such that it is in the
interval [0,1].
• Sun load fixed [%]: Similar to ”sun load adjustable,” the measure describes how
fixed solar performs. We have normalized this measure to be in the interval [0,1] as
well.
The vendor has converted the solar radiation into a load factor that describes how ad­
justable solar panels and fixed solar panels would perform at the specific location and
time of the node. Unfortunately, we do not have access to either the raw radiation data
or the vendor’s power curves that create this measure.
To limit ourselves, we will focus on the three metrological variables: wind speed, sun
load adjustable, and sun load fixed. These are the variable with the highest potential for
building an accurate model to predict the load factor. For future work, it could be interesting
to analyze, e.g., the provided temperature and wind direction and see how that affected
the models.
We will explore the metrological further in the upcoming two subsections. We have pro­
vided an overview of the subsection below:
1. Metrological Node Points. Here we will explore the distribution of onshore and
offshore locations and the location of node points in Western Europe.
2. Multicollinearity Among the Nodes. In this subsection, we will investigate the
multicollinearity between the nodes. This will be helpful when we later model the
data.
This section contains many figures describing the metrological data. We can find these
in Appendix B, but references to the figures will be made along the way as we proceed
through the section.
3.2.1
Metrological Node Points
The metrological data consist of data from 54 node locations in western Europe. We
can see the node locations in Figure 3.7. As we can see from the figure, it both include
onshore and offshore node location. The nodes originate from 4 different countries. The
three countries of interest for the case, namely France, Germany, and the United Kingdom,
but we also have four node locations for the Netherlands. We can see the distribution
of nodes and the countries in Figure 3.8. The specefic information about each node is
described in Table B.1.
3.2.2
Multicollinearity Among the Nodes
This subsection will investigate the multicollinearity of the metrological data at the different
locations of nodes. When encountering a variable with a high correlation to one or more
independent variables, the phenomenon is multicollinearity. It describes the level to which
we linearly predict one independent variable from the others with a firm degree of accuracy.
We can calculate the measure using Equation 3.4.

Table 3.2: Where we can find the correlation matrix heatmap for each of the three coun­
tries. In each heatmap, we only used the internal nodes in the country. For each node, we
only consider a single feature. We have marked the wind speed at offshore and node loca­
