and solar load fixed for offshore nodes marked with a red­blue color gradient.
In general, the solar load, both the fixed and the adjustable, have a higher correlation
than the wind speed. The pattern is due to the fact they solar generation depends on
night and day. At night the solar load is zero for all nodes while it at day differs. That
means approximately half the time the load factor at all node locations is equal, leading
to a higher correlation than wind speed that differs during all 24 hours of day regardless
of night and day.
While the figures referenced in Table 3.2 show intricate plots describing the correlation in­
side each country for each metrological variable, it does not show the correlation across
the countries.
Therefore, we have also produced correlation heatmaps for all nodes
across country borders. We can find the reference to these figures in the list below for
each climate variable.
• Wind speed: Figure B.13
• Solar load adjustable: Figure B.14
• Solar load fixed: Figure B.15
In the figures references above, the plot is the nodes grouped by country. The offshore
node is marked as well as the onshore nodes for each country. The figures contain much
information, but the core is that correlation exists across borders. For instance, the wind
speed variable is correlated across the offshore nodes in Germany, Netherlands, and
United Kingdom.
The Problem with Multicollinearity
When there are many correlated variables in the linear regression model, their coefficients
can become poorly determined and exhibit high variance. E.g., a wildly large positive
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
33
==PAGE:44==
coefficient on one variable can be canceled by a similarly large negative coefficient on its
correlated cousin. This is problematic from a global interpretation standpoint. A decrease
in one variable may offset an increase in another variable, so the combined effect negates
each other. However, that does not necessarily affect the predictions of the linear model.
A model with high multicollinearity presents can still do a decent job at predicting the target
variable.
With an in­depth description of the available data fresh in mind data, we will now proceed
to the section 3.3 and present the case problem more formally than we did in section 1.2.
3.3
Case Problem
The DAT wants to use a period of data where we have an overlap of load factor data
and metrological data to build a machine learning model to predict the historical load
factor. Recall that we have the capacity and generation data and load factor data for
01/01/2015­31/12/2019 and metrological data for 01/01/2000 ­ 31/12/2019. Thus we will
use the overlapping period of 01/01/2015­31/12/2019 to build a model that can predict
the historical load factor before 2015 (01/01/2000 ­ 31/12/2014). The idea is illustrated in
Figure 3.9. We will describe in greater detail how we split the data into a training and test
set and our general modeling approach in the next chapter 4.
Recall that we are estimating a load factor because it is a ratio between the generation
and capacity. The idea is that the ratio generalizes to the periods before 2015 when the
capacity and generation were smaller. However, as both capacity and generation are less
for previous years, the ratio should remain the same under similar metrological conditions.
The idea is that if we can estimate the load factor, 2000­2020, we can project them onto
future years for scenario analyses. E.g., if the year 2002 have similar wind conditions as
the year 2022, we have an idea of what the generation in 2022 will be because we know
the capacity.
We need to build models for each renewable energy type: offshore wind, onshore wind,
and solar for France, Germany, and the United Kingdom. However, the DAT had a diffi­
cult time deploying the models they created because the Analyst did not understand the
models and thus not trusted them.
In the upcoming section 3.4 we will explore what the Analysts perceive as an interpretable
model that solves the case problem. Essentially it is a list of requirements that align with
their expectation of the energy market and their expectations of feature influence. We
identified the list through interviews with both an Analyst at Ørsted but primarily through
interviews with Data Scientists at the DAT.

3.4
Stakeholders: The Analysts’ Requirements to the Model
An interpretable machine learning model has to obey a domain­specific set of constraints
to be more easily understood by its stakeholders [Rud19]. It is part of what define the
relevancy of the model choice. We have introduced the stakeholders in section 2.4 in
the light of the literature review. However, we have not talked about the stakeholders
interpretability constraints to the choice of model.
From online interviews/meetings with Ørsted, we have identified that the Analysts have
five requirements for a model to be perceived as interpretable4. We have described the
five constraints in the list below. We will learn that the five constraints are, in fact, intrinsic
interpretable methods.
C1 The model have to be linear. The Analysts find the linear model to be the most
interpretable model that solves the case problem. The linearity of the learned rela­
tionship makes the interpretation easy. Furhtmore a linear model, when it fulfills all
model assumptions, ensures modularity. I.e., An increase of feature xi by one unit
increases the prediction for y by βi units when all other feature values remain fixed.
Recall Modularity helps us solve (T1), (T2), and (T4).
C2 Specific power curve transformations have to used when modeling the wind
load factors. The Analysts know that the power generation of wind turbines is not
linearly dependent on wind speed. Instead, the relationship forms an ”s­curve.” To
ensure that we can still model it linearly, the Analysts want us to use a specific power
curve transformation. The idea is to map the wind speed to what we believe the es­
4We did not find additional model constraints with the Traders.
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
35
==PAGE:46==
timated relative power generation would be. We can see the power transformation
for offshore wind marked with blue and onshore wind marked with green and the
difference between the two in Figure 3.10. We have shown the effect of the power
transformation in Figure 3.11, which consists of two plots. We see the measured
wind speeds in the left plot, measured at node 53, for 2015­2018 on the x­axis and
the corresponding offshore load factor, for the United Kingdom on the y­axis. The
location of node 53 is the North Sea, close to the east coast of England. Here we
see that the observations generally form an ”s­curve.” 5 In the plot on the right, we
show the wind speed after we have applied the power curve transformation. After
the transformation, we obtain a much more linear relationship. The power curve
transformation is an example of how the intrinsic interpretable method feature en­
gineering increases the predictive accuracy of linear models. At the same time, it
does not harm interpretability because the Analysts are so familiar with the trans­
formation. The transformation ensures a better understanding of the features and
their influence. Thus it help us solve (T3) and (T4).
C3 The sum of the weights associated with each feature has to sum to one. When
we use power curve feature transformation, we implicitly incorporate prior knowl­
edge about how wind affects the load factor. For simplicity, let us assume we only
use one node, e.g., node 53, and the associated wind speed to predict a country’s
offshore wind load factor, e.g., the United Kingdom’s. The power curve transforma­
tion translates the entire feature space into a load factor. The assumption is that the
power curve transformation holds all the information ­ including an intercept. Thus
the Analysts believe the weight associated with the feature should be 1. If we con­
sider more features, e.g., all offshore nodes in the United Kingdom, then the sum
of features needs to sum to one. That way, we obtain a weighted sum of the power
transformation. Note that the same thing applies to the solar load factor. However,
instead of transforming solar radiation, the vendor who provides the metrological
data has already provided power transformations for adjustable solar panels and
fixed solar panels. Recall that we do not have access to solar radiation but only the
power curve estimates. This constraint help to solve task (T3) and (T4).
C4 The convex combination of features needs to make physical sense. The Ana­
lysts want the weight associated with parameters in the linear regression to be posi­
tive. Therefore, we are trying to predict a fraction between 0 and 1 (or a percentage
between 0­100) using variables that we expect to affect the prediction positively. I.e.,
it is natural to assume that when the wind blows at high speed, the load factor should
be estimated higher than when the wind speed is slow. This constraint ensures that
the features and their influence are better understood (T3), (T4).
C5 The choice of nodes in the model has to be native to what it is predicting.
The Analysts believe that the nodes used in the model need to be close to where
the generation takes place. That means nodes within a country should be used
only to predict load factors for that country. We should only use offshore nodes
to predict offshore load factors and vice versa for onshore nodes. Although the
underlying reason for this is different, it is a way to enforce sparsity to the model.
Recall sparse models allow for a more comfortable examination of weights in a linear
model. With few features we can easier detect arbitrary behavior (T2) and reason
about counterfactuals (T4, T5).
If we satisfy the above constraints in our choice of the model, we assume we achieve
5Generally, it does form an ”s­curve,” but we also see some observation that deviates from this path.
36
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:47==
high descriptive accuracy. However, all these constraints make it very difficult to create
a model with high predictive accuracy. The main challenge of this thesis is to propose
models that are simple enough to be easily understood by the Analysts while they maintain
high predictive accuracy.

3.5
Summary
In this chapter, we described the data we will use to develop our machine learning model.
We described both the features, i.e., the metrological data and the targets, i.e., the ca­
pacity and generation data that together form our target load factor. We formulated the
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
37
==PAGE:48==
case problem in the light of described data. Furthermore, we examined the Analysts’
constraints to an interpretable model. We can compress the chapter’s findings into the
following list:
• It was possible to extract generation data from ENTSO­E Transparency Platform for
all countries and all renewable types after 01/01/2015.
• The initial calculated load factor estimates do not align with governmental statistics,
particularly the difference for the offshore load factors for Germany and the United
Kingdom were immense.
• Under the assumption that the government statistics are correct, the source of error
is most likely due to the capacity data being wrong for Germany and the ENTSO­E
generation data being wrong for the United Kingdom.
• The algorithm that re­fits the capacity ensures load factor estimate more in line with
the governmental statistics. However, it is not perfect, nor does it solve the issue
with the renewable generation data for the United Kingdom.
• The metrological data is measured hourly for each of 54 node locations, and data
is available from 01/01/2000 ­ 31/12/2019.
• The metrological data for nodes located close together exhibits high multicollinearity.
• The metrological data, in general, have high autocorrelation.
• We need to build a machine learning model based on the period 01/01/2015 ­ 31/12/2019
to predict the past load factors (01/01/2000 ­ 31/12/2014).
• The Analysts have five constraints to what they perceive as an interpretable model.
• If they are all fulfilled, we achieve a model with the best possible descriptive accuracy,
but the predictive accuracy will most likely be limited.
38
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:49==
4
Modeling
In this chapter we will build models to solve the case problem. We will only consider
the models’ predictive accuracy in this chapter. The only descriptive accuracy we will
consider is whehter the models comply the with Analysts’ constraints. The theory behind
the selected model is elaborated in the supporting Appendix A. As we present the models
we will refences where we can read the underlying theory.
The overal idea of this chapter is to build simple models at first and then gradually add
complexity. This way we hope to show the Analysts what we gain in terms of predictive
accuracy for more complex models. In the next chapter 5 we will analyze what we lose
for using more complex models in terms of descriptive accuracy.
This chapter is composed of a total of four sections described below.
1. Machine Learning Strategy. In the first section we will acquaint ourselves with our
overall machine learning strategy. Here we will describe how we will get an unbiased
estimate of the predictive accuracy. How we go around tunning our hyperparamters
in our model. As well as what features we will use to predict the load factor.
2. Models In this section, we will present 18 models we will use to solve the case
problem. That includes both linear and non linear models what differs most in the
models is the number of nodes we use. Here we differentiate between a three­level
node typology. We can read the underlying theory behind the models in Appendix A.
3. Predictive Accuarcy Results In the third section we will present the predictive
accuracies of the 18 model for each country and each renewable type.
4. Summary. Finally we will end the chapter with a section that summarizes the key
point found in the chapter.
4.1
Machine Learning Strategy
This section will describe our machine learning strategy. We will go through the following
three subsections:
1. Measuring Predictive Accuracy. This subsection will describe how we will mea­
sure the models’ unbiased estimate of the predictive accuracy.
2. Modeling Tunning. This subsection will describe how we perform hyperparameter
tunning for the selected models.
3. Feature ussage. In this subsection, we will learn how we will use the nodes’ metro­
logical data.
Let us dive into the first subsection.
4.1.1
Measuring Predictive accuracy
We are trying to predict the fraction load factor = generaiton
capacity . As the capacity and generation
are non­negative and the capacity is always greater or equal than the generation, the load
factor is always a number in the interval [0, 1], that is comparable to a percentage. While
the models we will build tries to predict a number between zero and one, we will report the
predictive performance as a percentage. We assume a percentage is more interpretable
for stakeholders at Ørsted than a fraction.
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
39
==PAGE:50==
To assess the generalization error of the models, i.e., how well we can expect our models
to perform on new, unseen data, we will split the data into a training and a test set. The
training set will consist of data from 01/01/2015 ­ 31/12/2018, and the test will consist of
data from 01/01/2019 ­ 31/12/2019. We have visualized the period of available data in
Figure 4.1. We will use the training set to fit and tune the selected model. We will lock the
test away until all model fitting/tuning is complete. As the trained models have not utilized
the test set in their training we can use the test set to obtain an unbiased estimate of the
trained models.

4.1.2
Modeling Tunning
We will combine hyperparameter tuning and model selection with the use of nested cross­
validation. The method involves using k­fold cross­validation where we fit a model on
all folds and evaluate the fit on the holdout fold. We will refer to the aggregate of folds
used to train the model as the ”modeling dataset” and the held­out fold as the ”validation
dataset.” In each modeling dataset, we try out a set of hyperparameters in grid search
or random search to find the optimal settings for the model evaluated on the validation
dataset. Nested cross­validation helps us find the set hyperparameters that are best at
achieving the desired model behavior. Once we identify the optimal set of hyperparame­
ters, we refit the final model with this set on all the data. The refitted model is the model
we evaluate on the test set from Figure 4.1.
Depending on the choice of model and the number of parameters, we will use random
search or grid search. In general, we will use 100­fold to evaluate the hyperparameters.
Normally this is seen a high number of folds1, however, we achieve a more accurate
confidence interval on our estimate of the generalization error. We will also report the
mean cross­validation error, and the 5’th quantile and 95’th quantile error across the 100
folds. All predictive accurcies reported are the Mean Squared Error (MSE) loss function.
4.1.3
Feature ussage
We recall that the metrological data we will use to predict the load factor comes from 54
node points in Western Europe provided by an external vendor. The vendor has measured
1As a rule of thumb, data scientists tend to use 10­folds to limit the number of computations. It was only
possible to train complex black box models with 100­fold cross­validation because DTU Computing Center
makes high­performance computing resources available. We have tuned all models on the Central DTU HPC
Cluster (LSF 10). For more info: https://www.hpc.dtu.dk/?page_id=2520
40
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:51==
the temperature, wind speed, wind direction and published the power curved transformed
solar loads for adjustable and fixed solar panels at each node point for 2000­2019.
To limit ourselves, we will only consider a subset of the original features: Solar load ad­
justable, solar load fixed, and wind speed with possible power curve transformation. If the
model is linear, we will use the power curve transformation. If it is non­linear, we will use
the wind speed directly. When we are building models for estimating the solar load factor,
we consider either solar load adjustable or solar load fixed, never both. Hence we will
provide two models for estimating the solar load. When we build models for estimating
the wind load factor for both offshore and onshore, it will be clear from the context whether
we consider the wind speed of the power curve transformed wind speed.
For each node, we will only use one of the recorded features in our models. If we consider
multiple nodes, we will consider multiple features but only one feature for each node.
4.2
Models
Essentially we will solve the case problem by considering the following three different
types of models:
1. Linear regression models with possible constraints or possible regularization. We
use the possible constraints to ensure that the models can comply with the Analysts’
interpretability constraints. We can add regularization to deal with multicollinearity
and enforce sparsity. We have elaborated on the theory behind the linear model
and how we can impose the Analysts’ constraints and regularization in section A.1.
This section also elaborates on the theory behind the mean benchmark model and
the multivariate benchmark model we will also use. We will utilize the wind power
curve transformation to make the relationship between the nodes’ wind speed fea­
ture more linear to the onshore/offshore wind load target.
2. EXtreme Gradient Boosting models (XGBoost). XGBoost is a non­linear state­of­
the­art black­box machine learning algorithm for tabular data. We will use this model
to show how great predictive performance we can achieve. Because of non­linearity,
we will not utilize the power curve transformation when we try to predict the offshore
and onshore wind load factors, but we will rather use the raw wind speed. The theory
behind XGBoost is described in section A.2.
3. Generalized Additive Model (GAM). We describe GAMs as a flexible non­linear
extension of the Generalized Linear Model (GLM). The model provides both high
predictive accuracy for non­linear data because of its flexibility but keeps high inter­
pretability. When we apply the GAM, we will not use the power curve transformation
for the wind because the GAM can capture a non­linear relationship automatically.
We have described the theory behind GAMs in section A.3
We will fit 18 models for the three countries and each of three types of renewables. All
18 models are of the model types mentioned above, but they differ in terms of the nodes
we use. Note, we will fit the solar load 2 × 18 as we will differentiate between the feature
that describes the load for fixed solar panels and the load for adjustable solar panels.
Furthermore, we differentiate in the linear models regarding what constraint/regularization
we impose.
We differentiate between the choice of nodes we use in our models according to a three­
level typology we will denote the nodes typology. The upcoming subsections are devoted
to describing each level of the typology and subsection devoted to summarizing how mod­
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
41
==PAGE:52==
els comply with the Analysts’ interpretablity constraints. We can read a short description
of each subsection in the list below.
1. Single­Node Models. In the first level of the typology, we will only use a single node
and the appropriate feature to build our models.
2. Country­Shore­Nodes Models. In the second level, we will use multiple nodes;
however, we limit our use such that we only use the nodes located in the country’s
territory to predict that country’s load. Furthermore, we will only use onshore nodes
to predict onshore load and offshore nodes to predict offshore load.
3. All­Nodes Models. We have devoted the third section to the final level of nodes
typology. At this level we will not limit our node use. That means we will use all 54
nodes (with automatic feature selection).
4. The Models and the Analysts’ Constraints. In the fourth subsection we will see
how the models from the three level nodes typology comply with Analysts’ con­
straints.
4.2.1
Single­Node Models
In the first level of the typology, we will only use a single node to predict a country’s renew­
ables load factor. We will consider six different single­node models. We have described
the six models in the list below. Note the 3­letter model code; we will use this code to
refer back to the model.
SN1 Mean benchmark model. In the mean model, we guess the target’s mean value. We
have included this model as a benchmark comparison to other models. This model
does not use any features; however, we categorized it with the single­node model
as the two other categories were less obvious. We have formulized the mean model
in subsection A.1.3
SN2 Linear regression model without an intercept with emposed constraints such that the
model coefficients sum to one. That way the model satisfy the four out five of Ana­
lysts’ constraints: (C1), (C2), (C3) and (C4) the model can additionally satisfy (C5) if
we select an native node. We have described how we can empose the constraints
to ensure the compliance with the constrainst in subsection A.1.2.
SN3 Linear regression model with an intercept and emposed constraints. The model
essentially the same as (SN2) the difference is the model has an intercept.
SN4 Linear regression model with an intercept. In this model, we fit a linear regression
using maximum likelihood; thus, the model does not satisfy the constraint (C3): (The
sum of the weights associated with each feature has to sum to one). However, the
model still satisfy three out five constraint namely (C1), (C2) and (C4).
SN5 XGBoost model is a state­of­art non­linear machine learning model. The theory be­
hind the model is described in section A.2. The model satisfies none of the Analysts’
interpretability constraints.
SN6 GAMs are a flexible generalized linear model. The theory behind the model is de­
scribed in section A.3. The model depends linearly on unknown smooth function on
a predictor that can often are nonlinear. The models thus satisfy (C1)2.
2While the model is technically linear, we have not verified whether the Analysts perceive the GAM as a
linear model.
42
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:53==
We select the node that ensures the best fit by iterating over all 54 nodes. We pick the
node with the lowest mean 100­fold cross­validation error. The idea is illustrated in Fig­
ure 4.2. Here we consider model SN3 and the target: the UK’s offshore wind load factor.
In the Figure 4.2 we see the mean cross­validation, as well as the 5’th and 95’th error
quantile for each of 54 nodes. Node 47, which is not even an offshore node (though lo­
cated close to the shore), has the lowest cross validation error so we pick it as our single
node for model (SN3). With this method, there is no guarantee to select a node that com­
plies with the Analysts’ constraint (C5): Native nodes. However, if it is very important for
the Analysts to satisfy this constraint, we could exhange some predictive performance
and choose the offshore node 55 instead.
The process of trying out all 54 nodes and selecting the one with the smallest mean cross­
validation error have we applied to each of the models SN2­SN6 each target.

4.2.2
Country­Shore­Nodes Models
We will only use the nodes native to the load factor we are trying to predict with the country­
shore­nodes models. So, e.g., if we are trying to predict the offshore load factor for the
UK, we will only use the offshore nodes in British territory. For the second level of the
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
43
==PAGE:54==
node typology, we will consider eight different models. Note for the models (CS5)­(CS8)
we will consider three different level of sparsity. We have described the eight models in
the list below.
CS1 Multivariate benchmark model without an intercept. In this model we fix the co­
efficients the same value such that they sum to one. The model is formulized in
subsection A.1.4.
CS2 Multivariate benchmark model with an intercept. In this model, we fit the intercept
using the ordinary least squares and fix the coefficients with the same value such
that the model’s weights plus intercept sum to one. The model shares the same
theory as (CS1) and is formalized in subsection A.1.4. We have included (CS1­
(CS2 as benchmarks for multivaraite models3.
CS3 Multivariate linear regression that satisfy all the Analysts’ constraints without an in­
tercept.
CS4 Multivariate linear regression that satisfy all the Analysts’ constraints with an inter­
cept. The two models (CS3) and (CS4) share the same theory as their single nodes
partner (SN2) and (SN3). How we impose the constraints are described in subsec­
tion A.1.2
CS5 Lasso regression with coefficients forced positive. We will consider 1000 different
levels of ℓ1 regularization with parameter α and force the coefficients positive in
this model. Out of the 1000 different ℓ1 regularization, we will pick three solutions
we find using three levels of the ”ℓ0­norm” we impose with parameter µ. We have
described the theory behind the lasso model, the ℓ0­norm, and the constraint that
forces the coefficients positive in subsection A.1.5. The three levels of ”ℓ0­norm” we
will consider are the following:
(a) µ = 0: We select the model that minimizes the mean cross­validation error.
(b) µ = 0.1: We allow for a higher mean cross­validation error in exchange for
more sparsity
(c) µ = 0.2: We trade even more predictive accuracy for more sparsity.
CS6 Lasso regression (without coefficients forced positive). Like the lasso model (CS5)
with coefficients forced positive, we also impose the three levels of ”ℓ0­norm” speci­
fied with parameter µ:
(a) µ = 0
(b) µ = 0.1
(c) µ = 0.2
CS7 XGBoost. The regularization we can apply to boosted regression trees is applied
to the leaf value and not feature coefficients like in the lasso regression technique.
Thus we do not get the additional feature sparsity we wish. Instead, we will intro­
duce sparsity ”manually.” To do so, we will use the non­zero features/nodes from
three different levels of ”ℓ0­norm” used (CS6) in the XGBoost model. Hence we
will consider three XGBoost with different amount of nodes/features. We will thus
consider three different feature usage for country­shores­nodes XGBoost.
(a) µ = 0
3The two multivaraite benchmark (CS1) and (CS2) models are included per request from the DAT.
44
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:55==
(b) µ = 0.1
(c) µ = 0.2
CS8 GAM. While there are methods to enforce sparsity in GAMs, it is generally a coom­
plicated process and it has not been adopted by the python library ”pygam” yet. So
instead, we will use the same manual scheme to impose sparsity as we used for
the XGBoost model CS7. I.e. we will use the nonzero features/nodes identified
for (CS6) with three levels of ℓ0­norm. We will thus consider three different feature
usage for country­shores­nodes GAM.
(a) µ = 0
(b) µ = 0.1
(c) µ = 0.2
4.2.3
All Node Models
In the thirds and final level of nodes typology, we do not constrain the choice of nodes.
We will use all nodes to predict a country’s specific renewable load factor but select the
best through enforced sparsity. At this level, we consider four different models. For all
four models, we will consider three different levels of sparsity.
AN1 Lasso regression model with coefficients forced positive. The models share the
same underlying theory as its country­shode nodes partner (CS5), except we do
not limit our use of nodes. We also consider three solutions found with three levels
of the ”ℓ0­norm”:
(a) µ = 0
(b) µ = 0.1
(c) µ = 0.2
AN2 Lasso Regression Model.
The models share the same undelying theory as its
country­shode nodes partner (CS6).
(a) µ = 0
(b) µ = 0.1
(c) µ = 0.2
AN3 XGBoost. We will use the non­zero features/nodes identified with lasso model AN2
for the three different levels of ”ℓ0­norm” used. We will thus consider three different
feature usage for all­nodes XGBoost.
(a) µ = 0
(b) µ = 0.1
(c) µ = 0.2
AN4 GAM. We will use the non­zero features/nodes identified with lasso model AN2 for
the three different levels of ”ℓ0­norm” used. We will thus consider three different
feature usage for all­nodes GAMs.
(a) µ = 0
(b) µ = 0.1
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
45
==PAGE:56==
(c) µ = 0.2

Table 4.1: How the models we have used to predict the load factor comply with the Ana­
lysts’ interpretability constraints. The interpretability constraints are described in detail in
section 2.4. Note the single node models (SN2)­(SN6) have been marked with a paren­
theses (  ). This is because if we choose the node with the highest predictive accuracy,
there is no guarantee we do not violate (C5). However, we can also limit our node search
and choose the node with the best predictive accuracy that satisfies the constraint. Also,
note that we have added a checkmark for the generalized additive model (GAM) at lin­
ear constraints. GAMs linearly depends on unknown smooth function for the features.
The unknown smooth function can capture non­linear effects. However, the GAM itself is
linear.
In the upcoming section 4.3 we will investigate the predictive accuracy of the 18 models
for each country and each renewables type.
4.3
Predictive Accuarcy Results
In this section, we will present the predictive accuracy of the models we have created. We
will consider both the mean squared error for the test and the average cross­validation
error. We have created a bar plot visualization for each country and each renewable type
that allows for easy comparison of the predictive accuracy. We have created two plots
for solar load, one for each of the solar features, namely the power curve for adjustable
and the power curve for fixed. The plot also contains information about the number of
4For the solar load, we fit 2 × 18 models as we differentiate between the two features power curve for
adjustable solar panels and power curve for fixed solar panels.
46
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:57==
features we utilize in each model5. We can see the 18 models predictive performance for
each renewable type for
• France in Figure 4.5,
• Germany in Figure 4.6, and
• The United Kingdom in Figure 4.7.
We can find more details on the models’ predictive performance if we consult Appendix E.
This appendix includes the tables with the data we have used to create the above ref­
erenced figures. The tables also include more performance statistics such as the 5’th
50’th, 95’th quantile predictive accuracy from the 100 fold cross­validation scheme, and
the mean absolute error and coefficient of determination (R2) on the test set. Furthermore,
the table also contains information about the number of non­zero features used.
So what models have the best predictive accuracy, and what models have the worst?
There is no one answer. It depends on the country and the renewable type. One model
might perform better on a country and a renewable type and worse on another country/re­
newable set. However, when we investigate the predictive performance plots, we see
some patterns. The models tend to group in terms of their predictive performance and
complexity.
The list below summerizes the patterns identified in the figures: Figure 4.5, Figure 4.6,
and Figure 4.7 for each level of the node typology as well as patterns identified outside
the node typology in terms of predictive performance
1. Single node predictive performance patterns:
• The mean benchmark model (SN1), tends to have the worst predictive accu­
racy across all targets.
• The single node models that comply with most of the Analysts’ constraints
(SN2), (SN3) tend to perform a little better than the mean benchmark model.
However still pretty bad compared to the other models.
• The single node modes that that complies with a few of Analysts’ constraints:
(SN4), (SN5) performs pretty well. They tend to perform even better than the
multivariate models (CS1),(CS2), (CS3), (CS4).
2. Country shore nodes predictive performance patterns:
• The multivariate benchmark model and the models that comply all the Analysts’
constraints (CS1), (CS2), (CS3), and (CS4) tend to perform a little better than
SN1), (SN2), and (SN3) but worse than the remaing single node models.
• The lasso models (CS5), (CS6) tend to perform better than all single nodes
models and the (CS1)­(CS4).
• The flexible non­linear models XGBoost and GAM’s (CS7), (CS8) tend to pro­
vide yet another (modest) boost in predictive performance.
3. All nodes models predictive performance patterns:
• The lasso models (AN1), (AN2) tend to perform similar to flexible non­linear
models (CS7), (CS8).
5The number of features is a descriptive interpretability property. We have included it as it will reference
the figures in the next chapter from a descriptive interpretability point of view.
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
47
==PAGE:58==
• The all nodes models XGBoost and GAM’s models (AN3), & (AN4) tend to
increase the predictive performance yet another time. However many features
are needed to achieve this.
4. Other predictive performance patterns:
• The two targets GE wind offshore and UK wind offshore, that we identified
particular data misallginemnets for in chapter 3, exhibits the worst predictive
performance across almost all 18 models. We can identify this by examining
the y­axis describing the targets. For those two particular targets, the y­axis
spans a much larger scale. We can create a model for almost all other targets,
yielding a mean squared error both on the test set and mean cross­validation
of less than 0.5 [%]. For the two mentioned targets, we can barely get a mean
squared error below 1.0 [%].
• This also suggests a data quality issue for Germany wind offshore and the
United Kingdom wind shore.
To better understand the best and worst­performing models, we have selected the three
best­performing models regarding their predictive accuracy on the test set and their cross­
validation error across all targets. That means every of 4×3 plots presented in Figure 4.5,
Figure 4.6, and Figure 4.7 we have selected the three models with best predictive accu­
racy and counted how many time that model the same model type was among top three
in 4×3 plots. The results is presented in Figure 4.3. We have performed the same action
but for the models with the worst predictive accuracy. We have presented the results for
worst performing in Figure 4.4.
From the figures it is clear that the model choices (SN1), (SN2), (SN3), (CS1) and (CS2)
tend to yield low predictive accuracy. On the other the model choices (AN4), (AN3), tend
to yield high predictive accuracy paritcular when we select models with little sparsity.
To conclude, the non­linear models from the last level of node typology violate most of
the Analysts’ interpretability constraints but perform best in terms of predictive accuracy.
On the other hand, the simple single node models (SN2), (SN3) that are more in line
with Analysts’ interpretability constraints tend not to perform very well in term of predictive
accuracy. The multivariate models (C1), (C2) (C3) and (C3) that comply with all the analyst
constraints tend to perform a little better than (SN2), (SN3) but not by much.

4.4
Summary
In this chapter, we have learned about the models we use and their predictive accuracy.
The list below summarizes the key point from each section.
• Machine Learning Strategy (section 4.1):
– We estimated the predictive accuracy unbiased from a test set consisting of all
data 01/01/2019­31/12/2019
– We trained models on the data from 01/01/2015­31/12/2018
– We have tunned models with a 100 fold cross­validation scheme.
– We only use one feature per node point. For linear models, we will utilize the
wind power curve transformation. For the non­linear models, we will use the
wind speed directly.
• Models (section 4.2):
– We will consider a total of 18 models to predict each country’s renewable load
factor. We created 2 models for the solar load. We differentiate between the
feature that describes the power curve for adjustable solar panels and the
power curve for fixed solar panels provided by the external vendor.
– The 18 models are essentially just three different models: Linear models, ex­
treme gradient boosting models, and generalized additive models. The models
mostly differ in terms of the nodes we use.
– We created a three­level node typology to differentiate between the nodes the
models utilize. Here we differentiate between single nodes, nodes native to
each country, and no restriction on the use of nodes.
– We saw how the models comply with the analyst constraints:
* The linear models with few nodes comply with most constraints.
* The models that capture non­linear effects (XGBoost and GAMs) and use
multiple nodes comply with the fewest.
• Predictive Accuracy Results (section 4.3):
– We saw the predictive accuracy for all 18 models on all countries and all re­
newable types.
– The models that satisfy most of the Analysts’ constraints tend to have low ac­
curacy.
– The models that violate most of the Analysts’ constraints tend to have the high­
est predictive accuracy.
In the upcoming chapter 5 we will evaluate the predictive performance of the 18 models.

Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders

5
Post­hoc Analysis
In this chapter, we will evaluate the descriptive accuracy of the 18 trained model types
we presented in the previous chapter 4. The theory behind the models is described in
Appendix A. We can also find the theory behind the model­agnostic post­hoc method
SHAP we will use to increase the descriptive accuracy in the same chapter in section A.4.
Unlike the predictive accuracy we presented in the previous chapter 4 the descriptive
accuracy is much harder to evaluate and benchmark. We will analyze the 18 models
relative to their level in nodes­typology.
According to the DAT, the most important market for Ørsted is the offshore wind energy
market in the United Kingdom. We will hence present the post­hoc interpretation for 18
models describing that target. We will consider this target despite the fact that we identified
the underlying issue with data quality for that target in particular in chapter 3. While we
had proposed the refitting algorithm to solve the issue, there is room for investigated this
issue further.
This chapter is composed of six sections described below.
1. Descriptive Accuracy of the Single­Node Models. In this section we will evaluate
the models from the first level of the node typology we described in the previous
chapter 3. In other words we will evaluate model (SN1)­(SN6).
2. Descriptive Accuracy of the Country­Shore­Nodes Models. In the second sec­
tion we will analyze the descriptive accuracy of the second level of the node typology
i.e. the models where we adhere to the Analyst’s requirements that nodes we use in
the models need to be native to what they are predicting (interpretability constraint:
(C5)). We will analyze the descriptive accuracy of the models: (CS1) ­ (CS8).
3. Descriptive Accuracy of the All­Nodes Models. In this section we will evaluate
the models from the third and final level of the typology i.e. the models where we use
all the nodes (with automatic feature selection). However as the results align with
country­shore­nodes models from the previous section it will not be as extensive.
4. Post­Hoc Discussion This section will discuss the features reduction scheme and
its limitation and our thoughts on the Analysts’ interpretability constraints.
5. Descriptive and Predictive Accuracy Tradeoff. With an analyze of the descriptive
performance of the 18 models we will describe our thoughts on the tradeoff between
predictive accuracy and descriptive accuracy in the fifth section.
6. Summary In the sixth and final section we will summarize the key points found in
the chapter.
5.1
Descriptive Accuracy of the Single Node Models
This section will evaluate the descriptive accuracy of the single node models, i.e., the first
level of the node usage typology. This section consists of four subsections, where we
in each subsection have grouped the single node models that share common properties.
We describe the four subsections and the grouped models in the list below.
1. SN1. We will evaluate the the mean benchmark model in the first subsection.
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
55
==PAGE:66==
2. SN2, SN3, SN4 (Linear Models). In the second subsection, we will evaluate the
descriptive accuracy of the single node linear models.
3. SN5: Extreme Gradient Boosting (XGBoost). In the third subsection, we will
analyze the descriptive accuracy of the single node non­linear XGBoost model.
4. SN6: Generalized Additive Model (GAM). In the fourth and final subsection, we
will explore the descriptive accuracy of the single node GAM.
We achieve the best predictive accuracy for models (SN3), (SN5) (SN6) when we use
node 47. Likewise we ensure the best predictive accuracy for the models (SN2) (SN4)
when we use node 53. In Figure F.1 we can see the mean 100 fold cross validation error for
all 54 nodes when use (SN2)­(SN6) to predict the the United Kingdom wind offshore load
factor. The location of nodes 47 and 53 are displayed in Figure 5.1. Note that the node is
located onshore and not an offshore node (though it is located close to the North Sea and
node 53). Thus all the models (SN3), (SN4) (SN6) violates the Analysts’ fifth interpretable
constraint (C5). However, if we are willing to sacrifice some predictive accuracy, we could
use the node that ensures compliance with Analysts’ fifth interpretable constraint (C5).

5.1.1
SN1 (Mean Benchmark Model)
The benchmark model is arguably the model with the highest descriptive accuracy if we
disregard the fact that it in principle violates all the Analysts’ interpretability constraints.
The model is completely intrinsic interpretable; we guess the average load factor from
the target for the training data. In the context of the United Kingdom’s wind offshore load
factor. We have described the mathematical formula in Equation 5.1 below:
SN1 = ¯y
(5.1)
= 39.12 [%]
(5.2)
Despite the good descriptive accuracy, the model is not super interesting because it yields
the lowest predictive accuracy, as we saw in the previous chapter 4. The model is primarily
included so that we can compare the other models’ predictive accuracy relative to the
”dummy” mean model.
56
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:67==
5.1.2
SN2, SN3, SN4 (Linear Models)
All three models (SN2), (SN3), and (SN4) are linear model with a single feature. Model
(SN2) is the only model without an intercept. For this model, we predict the load factor
is equal to the power curve transformed wind speed. All models satisfy the analyst con­
straints: (C1), (C2), and (C4). Furthermore (SN2) and (SN3) also satisfies the Analysts’
third interpretability constraint (C3).
All the models (SN2)­(SN4) have high descriptive accuracy as they are globally intrinsic in­
terpretable. The single feature linear model is very sparse as they consist of 1­2 modules.
The feature engineered wind speed improves the predictive accuracy, and the transforma­
tion is well known to the Analysts. We deem that the power curve transformation does not
decrease the predictive accuracy. We can easily infer a prediction given an observation.
If we let N47PCWS and N53PCWS denote the offshore power curve transformed wind speed
at node 47 and node 53, we can describe the fitted models as follows1:
SN2(N53PCWS) = 1 × N47PCWS
(5.3)
SN3(N47PCWS) = 0.146 + 0.854 × N47PCWS
(5.4)
SN4(N53PCWS) = 0.146 + 0.824 × N47PCWS
(5.5)
We can inspect with a single plot what the model predicts across the feature space for the
single­nodes model. In Figure 5.2 we have plotted the three models’ predictions in wind
speed feature space and the power curve transformed feature space. From Figure 5.2
it is clear that we capture a non­linear relationship between wind speed and load factor.
With power curve transformation, we make the relationship more linear. If we inspect the
models in the wind speed domain, we see the difference between models most clearly.
The intercept difference between (SN2) and (SN3)­(SN4) is easy to spot. With (SN3)­
(SN4) we never predict a load factor lower than 14.6 % because of the intercept. With
(SN3), we could, in theory, predict a load factor of 100 %, however as the power curve
transformation never converts a given wind speed to more than 96 %, that is the maximum
that it predicts. For (SN4), we never predict more than 93.7 % as we scale the load factor
feature more.
Of the three models, we perceive model (SN2) to have the highest descriptive accuracy.
It is the only model that consists of a single module (the model does not have an inter­
cept); thus, it is the only model that utilizes the power curve transformation without any
scaling. Model (SN3) have slightly less descriptive accuracy as we scale the power curve
transformation, but unlike (SN4), the coefficients + the intercept still sum to one.
The two constraints (C3) and (C5) limit the predictive accuracy of the linear models. How­
ever, we can satisfy (C5) by changing the node we use for a node native to its predictions
in exchange for a little predictive accuracy (and in some cases we do not even need to
change the node). The thirds constraint (C3) is more difficult to deal with. It puts a hefty
toll on the predictive accuracy.
5.1.3
SN5: Extreme Gradiet Boosting (XGBoost)
Rather than using the power curve transformation, we can learn the non­linear relation­
ship between wind speed and the load factor through a non­linear model. XGboost is
an algorithm that captures non­linear relationships through the creation of decision trees.
Unfortunately, it is impossible to account for the prediction of an XGBoost model with a
simple mathematical formula like we could with the linear model. That would require we

analyzed a complex tree structure. However, as we only have a single feature, we can
plot the prediction across the feature space. We have done so in Figure 5.3.
From Figure 5.3 it is clear that XGBoost uses decision trees for its prediction. This is
visible because the predictions are pretty ”jumpy,” especially when the wind speed is in
the interval 18­20 m/s, at node 47. The jumpy behavior is due to a lack of observations.
The result is overfitting. For training data at node 47, we have registered 7876 (22.5 %)
observation with wind speeds between 5­7 m/s. For the period with really jumpy behavior
of 18­20 m/s we have only registered 199 (0.5 %) observations. If we discard the jumpy
behavior and look at the trend, we see the model captures a similar ”s­curve” like the one
we resemble with the power curve transformation if we examine the trend. In general,
the single nodal XGBoost has better predictive accuracy than the linear models. How­
ever, the captured jumpy relationship is probably not a good replacement for power curve
transformation in the eyes of Analysts. We doubt they would expect this ”jumpy” behavior.
It would be difficult to explain that the jumpy behavior is a product of the underlying tree
structure and the data. Furthermore, it is not very easy to articulate the model’s predic­
tions mathematically. We can not write up a simple formula for the prediction as we can
with the linear models.
At the moment, none of the Analysts’ constraints are satisfied. Only if we sacrifice some
predictive accuracy and use a British offshore node will the model satisfy just one of the
Analysts’ interpretability constraints (C5). Hence of all the single nodes model, we classify
SN5 to have the lowest descriptive accuracy of the single­node models.

5.1.4
SN6: Generalized Additive Models (GAMs)
GAMs propose a different approach to learn the non­linear relationships between the wind
speed and the load factor than the XGBoost. With GAMs, we can control the smoothness
of the non­linear relationship through the regularization parameter λ. We have tried 101
different λ between [1, 1000], and we picked the regularization parameter that ensured the
lowest mean cross­validation error across the 100 folds. However, we could impose more
smoothness with a higher λ value if the result aligned more with the Analysts’ expectations
for the learned power curve transformation.
With the single feature GAM we learn the non­linear relationship between wind speed and
load factor and how it constributes to the expected prediction instead. The fitted GAM for
SN6 using node 47 takes the form:
SN2(N47WS) = 48.8 + f(N47WS).
(5.6)
Where f(·) is a smooth function constructed from a set of 20 cubic splines forming a
basis. Like the XGBoost, f(·) is difficult to put into a straightforward mathematical formula.
However, we can plot the partial dependence for function f(N47WS). The PDP is plotted
in Figure 5.4. For simplicity, we have also plotted the prediction for the model across the
feature space in Figure 5.5. Note that the last plot is only possible to create for a single
feature model.
From Equation 5.6 we note that the mean is 48.81 %. That is a surprise because the mean
is different from the 39.12 % estimated with the mean benchmark model. That suggests
that Python library Pygam utilizes a different algorithm than the back­fitting algorithm we
presented in section A.3. Unfortunately, we could not identify the method that Pygam
utilizes or how it differs.
The single­node GAM model (SN6) tends to have the same predictive accuracy (if not a lit­
tle better) than the single node XGBoost model (SN5). However, the captured relationship
resembles the power curve transformation better than the jumpy prediction of (SN5). As
the found relationship act more as power curve transformation, which is already a common

5.2
Descriptive Accuracy of the Country­Shore­Nodes
Models
This section will evaluate the descriptive accuracy of the country­shore­nodes models. We
are considering the target United Kingdom’s wind offshore load factor as an example of a
target, but the findings presented in this section generalize to the other targets. With this
target, we will utilize all offshore nodes in United Kingdom’s territory. There is a total of 12
nodes. We have displayed their location in Figure 5.6. The country­shore­nodes models
are multiple feature models that all satisfy the Analysts’ fifth interpretability constraint (C5).
We cannot show the country­shore­nodes models’ predictions in a single plot like we could
with the single­node models. We will instead explain how the model arrives at its predic­
tions with an example. Here we will consider a single observation from the test set. More
specifically, we will consider the observation recorded on 28­01­2019 at 12:00:00. Ta­
ble 5.1 shows the measured wind speeds at the 12 different offshore nodes. At the same
time, the corresponding load factor for UK offshore was registered to be: 55.57 %.

same properties and devote a subsection to each group. We have grouped the eight
models into four groups. We describe the groups in the list below.
1. CS1, CS2, CS3, CS4: Linear Benchmark and Restricted Linear Models. The
Four listed country­shore­nodes­model share the fact that they all obey the Analysts’
third interpretability constraint (C3) ­ none of the other models from this nodes level
typology obey this constraint. We will not explain how the linear model arrives at
their prediction for observation 28­01­2019 at 12:00:00 as the method is the same
as for the lasso regression models.
2. CS5, CS6: Lasso Regression. The two models (CS5) and (CS6) are both lasso
regression models. For (CS5), we force coefficients to be positive, so it obeys the
Analysts fourth interpretability constraint (C4) by imposing a constraint. For (CS6)
we relax that constraint so we are not guaranteed to obey constraint (C4). For the
lasso models, we will consider three levels of regularization. We utilize the ℓ0 norm
described in Equation A.11 with µ = 0, µ = 0.1, µ = 0.2. For µ = 0, we select
the model that minimizes the cross­validation error. For all levels above µ = 0 we
enforce more sparsity by sacrificing predictive accuracy.
3. CS7: XGBoost. In this section, we will present the multiple feature XGBoost model.
We will explore how we can open the ”black­box” model with the use of SHAP. We
will consider the XGBoost that utilize the same features found for the lasso model
(CS6) with µ = 0.2. That way, the model is easier to explain because of the enforced
sparsity. The results however, generalizes to the less sparse models (CS6)µ=0 and
(CS6)µ=0.1 we ”just” have to keep more features in mind.
4. CS8: GAM. Finally, we will round off this section by presenting the multi­feature
GAM. We will also only consider the GAM with the same features identified by
(CS6) with µ = 0.2.

CS1, CS2, CS3, CS4: Mulivariate Linear Benchmark and Restricted
Models
All four models (CS1)­(CS4) satisfy all five constraints imposed by the Analysts (C1)­(C5).
Model (CS1) and (C4) are without an intercept and (CS2) and (CS4) are with an intercept.
The coefficient of the model plus the intercept sum to one (the Analysts third interpretability
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders

For the other two models (CS3) and (CS4), we find the weights by minimizing the mean
squared under the constraint that the weight plus the intercept have to sum to one. We

it led to different models, it does not affect the predictive accuracy. Across all targets
model (CS5) and (CS6) perform very similar in terms of predictive accuracy. We can
thus ensure higher descriptive accuracy as we ensure to satisfy the Analysts fourth inter­
pretability constraint C4. When we empose µ = 0.2 level of ℓ0 regularization, we trade
more sparsity for predictive accuracy and thus we increase descriptive accuracy. How­
ever, as the model does not satisfy the Analysts’ third interpretability constraint (C5), we

deem the lasso model to have less descriptive accuracy than the (C1)­(C4)3.
5.2.3
CS7: XGBoost
The black box machine learning algorithm can create state­of­the­art predictions for prob­
lems with tabular data. However, the model is challenging to interpret. Especially when
we add more than a single feature and start to harness the models’ predictive power, by
capturing interaction effects, it is impossible to show the model predictions across the
entire feature space as we could with (SN5).
We will ”open” the black­box XGBoost model CS7µ=0.2 with SHAP. The model CS7µ=0.2
utilizes the same features as the lasso model (CS6) with µ = 0.2 level of ℓ0 regularization.
In Figure 5.12, we can see four SHAP dependence plots, one for each node we utilize.
SHAP dependence plots are similar to partial dependence plots but account for the inter­
action effects present in the features. Furthermore, only in regions supported by data are
the SHAP values defined. In Figure 5.12 we have marked the wind speed observation at
28­01­2019 at 12:00:00 recorded at each of the four nodes and their corresponding SHAP
value. The SHAP values for the observation give us the marginal contribution to the pre­
diction. If we add the marginal contribution for the observation together with the expected
prediction E[f(X)], we obtain the prediction from our XGBoost model. We have visualized
the process in Figure 5.13 as an example of how we can obtain local interpretability with
XGBoost.
The robust theoretical background in cooperative game theory and the fast Tree­SHAP
algorithm is excellent for local and global interpretation of XGBoost models. However,
the SHAP dependence plot only explains how the XGBoost model works, not necessarily
how reality works (or expectation of reality work). We can see different variations of the
’s­curve’ in the SHAP dependence plots (with a creative mindset) that we know from the
power­curve transformation. Interaction effects drive the vertical dispersion of SHAP val­
ues at a single feature value. That means we do not get a one­to­many mapping we know
from the power curve transformation rather a many­to­many. Furthermore, the ’s­curve’
from the SHAP dependence plot exhibits jumpy behavior. So while there is ”s­curve” pat­
ten in the SHAP dependence plots we e.g. see N53WS = 13.39 m/s can yield marginal
contribution between 7­12 [%] to the predicted load factor. In other words, we do not get
an exact modular interpretation that generally improves the descriptive accuracy.
The country shore XGBoost model (CS7) tend to improve the predictive accuracy relative
to the linear model CS1­CS6. When we utilize the same features found in the lasso model,
we can manually impose sparsity in (CS7) to increase the descriptive accuracy. With
SHAP, we can interpret the model on a more modular level, as we can compose each
prediction into a set of marginal contributions. However, because of the model capture
interaction effects, we do not get an exact modular interpretation. We, therefore, deem
the model to have the lowest descriptive accuracy of the country shore models.
5.2.4
CS8: GAM
In this section we will consider the model (CS8)µ=0.2 i.e. the GAM model that utilize the
same features as lasso model (SN6) with a level µ = 0.2 of ℓ0 regularization. We can
describe the fitted (CS8)µ=0.2 with Equation 5.12 below:
CS8(X)µ=0.2 = 42.28 + f1(N53WS) + f2(N54WS) + f3(N55WS) + f4(N58WS).
(5.12)
3The lasso models cannot be compared one­to­one with the (C1)­(C4) to do so we should utilize the same
features as identified by the lasso model for the different levels of ℓ−0 regularization. However, as we only
expect the predictive accuracy to drop and descriptive accuracy is expected to increase because of sparsity,
we deemed this unnecessary.
66
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:77==
We have plotted the PDP between the four features and target variables in Figure 5.14.
The plot show how the function f1, f2, f3 and f4 affect the predictions. For linear models
such as the GAM, we can read the SHAP right of the partial dependence plot. We can
thus see each components’ marginal contribution to the prediction. In Figure 5.14 we have
also marked the wind speed observation at 28­01­2019 at 12:00:00 recorded at each of
the four nodes and their corresponding marginal contribution / SHAP value. Unlike the
XGBoost, we get full modularity by the design of the GAM. In other words, the ”power
curve” we learn for each feature has the one­to­many property that makes them easy to
interpret. A single wind speed recorded at one node has one and only one mapping to a
SHAP value. Suppose we add the marginal contribution for the observation together with
the expected prediction E[f(X)] we obtain the prediction from our GAM model. We have
visualized the process in Figure 5.15.
The smoothness of the functions is controlled with λj ∈{1, ..., p}. For the GAM with p >> 1
features, it is computationally infeasible to make a grid­search to find the set regularization
parameters λj ∈{1, ..., p} that minimizes the 100­fold cross­validation. Instead we have
considered a random search with 250 different constellations for λj ∈{1, ..., p} in interval
[1­1000]. For model the (CS8)µ=0.2 that consider the United Kingdom’s offshore wind load
factor as target the we find the set of λj’s the minimizes the cross­validation to be:
λ = [1.0292, 285.4524, 14.4954 26.2988, ]
(5.13)
The different levels for λi associated with each function fj partially explains why f1 is
so ”curvy” and why f2 is so smooth. The very curvy f1 does arguably not represent the
nature that the Analysts expect of power­curve. Instead, it should ideally represent an
”s­curve.” However, what if we could make each λi follow a smoother path? We will see
what happens to the partial dependences plot in the upcoming section if we increase λ
and what we lose/gain in predictive accuracy.
CS8: A more Smooth GAM by Manually Picking Regularization
We can force the functions fj that describe that GAMs partial dependence to become
more smooth by imposing more regularization with λ parameter. A smoother curve could
potentially capture the Analysts’ expectations better. Lets denote the GAM with extra
harsh regularization (λ_CS8)µ=0.2. As an example, we manually tried to fit a GAM with
the following regularization parameters:
λ = [500, 1000 200 200] .
(5.14)
We can see the resulting partial dependence plots in Figure 5.16. We have also marked
the wind speed observation on 28­01­2019 at 12:00:00. In Figure 5.17 we show how
the marginal contribution for the observation form the prediction. The fj are much more
smooth than compared to partial dependence plots of (CS8)µ=0.2 we displayed in Fig­
ure 5.14. They arguably capture the ”s­curve” the Analysts would expect better. Rather
than a fixed power curve, we learn an individual curve for each node and scale their im­
portance for predicting the entire country’s load factor based on data.
Imposing more regularization comes with a price (but a tiny one). For (CS8)µ=0.2 the mean
cross­validation error is: 0.8351 whereas the mean cross­validation error for heavier reg­
ularized GAM (λ_CS8)µ=0.2 is is 0.8364. A difference of 0.0013. The heavier regularized
GAM performs better on the test set. It scores 1.1461 where (CS8)µ=0.2 scores 1.1472.
In other words, it is a small price, if any, for imposing more regularization in this example.
However, that only makes sense if the Analysts support the idea of learning a specific
power curve at each node and discard their fixed curve.
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
67
==PAGE:78==
The grid search technique is programmed to select the model with the best predictive
performance and, it is challenging to create a scheme that also ensures that found re­
lationship looks like a power curve transformation. That is why we have imposed the
regularization manually.
The descriptive accuracy for (CS8) is higher than (CS7) while they have approximately
the same predictive accuracy. With the GAM, we get complete modularity. Furthermore,
we do not need to access the post­hoc model­agnostic method to improve the descriptive
accuracy. For the GAM, the modularity comes by design (it is model­specific methods).
Furthermore, if we impose more regularization into the model, we can mimic the ”s­curve”
relationship between the wind speed and the load factor that might follow the Analysts’
expectations. If the Analysts does buy the idea of the individual learned power curve,
then the model probably has less descriptive accuracy than the lasso models CS5­CS6
because it violates both interpretability constraint (C2) and (C3). However, if they do buy
the idea, the model satisfies all the constraints, which would give it the same descriptive
accuracy as (CS5) but with much higher predictive accuracy.

5.3
Descriptive Accuracy of the All­Nodes Models
As the four all­nodes models (AN1)­AN4) are the same model types as the country­shore
node model (CS5)­(CS8) with the only difference that we do not constraint the node usage,
such that we disobey the Analysts’ fifth interpretability constraint (C5), we will not describe
(AN1)­AN4) extensively. For all the four model we have considered three levels of ℓ0
regularization: µ = 0, µ = 0.1, µ = 0.2. We can describe the general recipe for fitting the
all­nodes models with the following list:
1. Fit the lasso model (AN1, (AN2) (with the possible constraint that ensures positive
features) with all nodes.
2. Reduce the number of features with the appropriate level of ℓ−0 norm.
3. Evaluate the descriptive/predictive accuracy of the sparse lasso model.
4. With the identified set of sparse features, we can proceed to fit non­linear models
(AN1), (AN2)
5. Evaluate the descriptive/predictive accuracy of the non­linear models.
In general, we can squeeze out more predictive accuracy with the all­nodes model. How­
ever, this is typically only ensured if consider a mild level of ℓ0 regularization (µ = 0,
µ = 0.1). On the other hand, if we impose heavy ℓ−0 regularization, we typically end
with the same predictive accuracy as the country­shore nodes with a comparable set of
nodes.
The all­nodes models are less descriptive accurate than their country­shore­nodes mod­
els’ partner. For the all­nodes to perform better, we typically need many more nodes, and
hence we will violate the Analyts’ fifth interpretability constraint (C5), and it will be difficult
to interpret because of the lack of sparsity. Apart from that the models share descriptive
properties as we discussed for country­shore nodes model (CS5)­(CS8) in section 5.2.
5.4
Post­Hoc Discussion
This section will discuss the features reduction scheme and its limitation and our thoughts
on the Analysts’ interpretability constraints.
5.4.1
Issues with the Feature Reduction Scheme
Using the lasso regression model, we can reduce the number of features by imposing
more ℓ1 regularization with parameter α. Here we can consider all nodes or just the
nodes native to what we are predicting oor the third constellation. We can find the op­
timal level between the number of features and the predictive accuracy by imposing ℓ0
regularization. Alternatively, we can directly select the level of α that leaves us satisfied.
With the identified non­zero features, we can proceed to create a non­linear model that
improves accuracy with identified nodes.
The scheme is efficient for eliminating features. It gives us an option to select models
where we comprise predictive accuracy for more sparsity. The enforced sparsity improves
the descriptive accuracy of all models. In Figure F.3 we show how we can select nodes for
the model: AN1) considering the target: United Kingdom’s Offshore wind load factor.
While this scheme is efficient for eliminating features ­ it is not a perfect method. The
lasso, however, only assigns importance to features that a linear to the target. That means
non­linear potentially important features usually shrinks to zero, and we do not harness
their power in the non­linear model. So if the provided power­curve transformation is
72
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:83==
wrong or the non­linear dependence is underlying different, we potentially discard valuable
information.
5.4.2
Thoughts on The Analysts’ Contraints
C1 Linear model. Using linear regression models restricts the predictive performance.
However, if we use GAM’s, where the target depends linearly on unknown smooth
functions (that potentially looks like power curves), we have not see limitation to the
predictive performance.
C2 Use of power curve. The power curve transformation makes the relationship be­
tween the target and features more linear. The feature transformation scheme helps
to improve the predictive accuracy. We cannot do anything about the two power
curve transformations for the solar load (the power curve for adjustable and fixed
panels) as we do not have access to raw solar radiation data, and we do not know
the power curve transformation scheme. However, for the wind power curve, we
can question whether or not a better curve exists. We saw that both the GAM and
XGBoost captured power curve­looking patterns directly from the wind speed data
different from the power curve proposed by the Analysts.
C3 The sum of features equal one. We can only obey this constrain if we consider
linear regression models. If we consider such models, that constraint puts a hefty
toll on the predictive performance. The reason is that the power curve is too opti­
mistic about its estimation of the maximum load factor. If we consider the power
curve transformation for the wind speed, from Figure 3.10, we see it transformation
might be too optimistic. Predicting 96 % for an entire country when the wind speed
is between 15­21 m/s for offshore winds are arguably too high. The load factor
transformation seems like a good relationship for a single wind turbine; however, for
an entire countries fleet of thousands wind turbines, the relationship seems like a
simplification. The power curve transformation does, e.g., not capture the fact that
at any point, we need to shut down wind turbines for maintenance or repair, which
drag the country’s load factor down.
C4 Weight make physical sense i.e.
positive weights.
This constraint has not
proven to limit the predictive accuracy in practice.
C5 Native Nodes. The native nodes restrict the number of features which limits the
predictive accuracy. However, the native nodes are typically the nodes that possess
the most information for explaining a country’s load factor. For a sparse model, it
can be meaningful only to consider native nodes to limit the search.
5.5
Descriptive and Predictive Accuracy Tradeoff
We have benchmarked the descriptive accuracy of the model relative to the other models
at the same level of the nodes typology according to the methods we described in chap­
ter 2. As a result, we perceive models of the same type to have the highest descriptive
accuracy if they live in a single­node level and the lowest if they live in the all­nodes level.
We consider the three metrics in the PDR framework: descriptive accuracy, predictive
accuracy, and relevancy. Measuring the predictive accuracy of the 18 models is straight­
forward. It is possible but a complex challenge to benchmark the descriptive accuracy of
the 18 proposed models relative to model type and node typology. We cannot put a num­
ber on the descriptive accuracy. It is a qualitative analysis. Finding the optimal tradeoff
between predictive accuracy and descriptive accuracy through the last metric relevancy
is even more challenging.
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
73
==PAGE:84==
We have shown what we lose and gain in terms of predictive and descriptive accuracy
for 18 models, but it is up to the Analysts, with possible counseling from DAT at Ørsted,
to decide on the tradeoff. The GAM show some auspicious characteristics, especially if
we impose heavy regulation with parameter λ. That way, we smooth the estimate, so we
obtain curves that mimic the behavior of power curves; however, whether the Analysts
accept that behavior remains an open question.
5.6
Summary
In this chapter we analyzed the descriptive accuracy of the models for each level of nodes
ussage typology with UK’s offshore wind load factor as the example (the findings gener­
alizes to the other targets). We discussed the feature reduction scheme and the analysts
constraint. Finally we discussed the the predictive/descriptive accuracy tradeoff. The list
below summarizes the key point from each section.

follows (excluding the benchmark model (SN1):

• Post­Hoc Discussion (section 5.4):
– There is room for improvement in the feature reduction scheme. For example,
the current scheme might not capture valuable alternative non­linear explana­
tory power.
– The Analysts’ constraints primarily limit the predictive accuracy. However, not
all constraint means worse predictive accuracy.
Some constraint improves
the descriptive accuracy without harming the predictive accuracy. However,
the third interpretability constraint (C3) put a hefty toll on the predictive perfor­
mance, particularly with a provided wind power curve.
• Descriptive and Predictive Accuracy Tradeoff (section 5.5):
– We have evaluated the models’ predictive accuracy and descriptive accuracy,
but it is up to the Analysts to decide on the tradeoff.
– We would recommend the DAT to try and sell the idea of a sparse multivariate
GAM as we believe it potentially can obey the Analysts’ constraint while yielding
high predictive accuracy.
