6
Conclusion and Future Work
6.1
Conclussion
Enterprises face a crucial challenge on their journey for adopting AI: how to balance
the predictive and descriptive accuracy. The choice between simple white­box models
and complicated black­box models. Ørsted is no exception. In this thesis we defined
interpretability and identified methods to improve the interpretability for ML models for a
case the DAT at Ørsted recently faced.
We defined interpretability to be the combinations of both global and local interpretability.
We identified three metrics predictive accuracy, descriptive accuracy and relevancy to
evaluate the performance of ML model in the lights of interpretability. We analyzed the
stakeholders for the case and their underlying objectives for interpretability. We identified
five problem­specific interpretability constraints the stakeholders expect the models to
satisfy.
Based on a quantive analysis, we analyzed the predictive accuracy of 18 different mod­
els. Here we considered the unbiased test­error where we used a large test set, and the
cross­validation error. Through a qualitative analysis of the models, we analyzed their
descriptive accuracy. We benchmarked the 18 models’ descriptive accuracy relative to
other models at the same level of the three­level nodes typology and for the same model
types across the typology. In the first level, we considered single node models. Their high
sparsity ensures high descriptive accuracy relative to the two other levels. In the second
level, we considered nodes combinations that satisfy the Analysts fifth interpretability con­
straint (C5). More features ensured a better predictive accuracy. However, we had to use
more interpretability methods to improve the descriptive accuracy of these models. For
the multivariate XGBoost, we improved the descriptive accuracy by utilizing the post­hoc
model­agnostic method SHAP. In the last level of nodes typology, we did not restrict the
node usage. At this level, we saw yet an improvement in predictive accuracy. However,
this was typically only achieved by considering many more nodes than the country­shore
nodes models. More features make the models harder to interpret it thus lead to a drop
in the descriptive accuacy.
While we have measured the predictive accuracy and evaluated the descriptive accuracy
of the 18 models, we have not decided on the best trade­off that depends on the last metric:
the relevancy of the problem. We have presented what we lose in terms of descriptive
accuracy and what we gain in terms of predictive accuracy when we gradually increase
the model complexity. The DAT and the stakeholders need to settle on the trade­off on
their own. We, however, recommend the DAT to consider a sparse GAM with heavy
regularization. That model can potentially satisfy all five interpretability constraints, and
its modular nature makes it highly interpretable while it has similar predictive accuracy to
an XGBoost model with the same number of features.
We analyzed underlying data for the case extensively. We identified data misalignment
between the ENTSO­E generation data and governmental statistics ­ particularly for the
United Kingdom offshore wind generation­and between the provided capacity data and
governmental statistics, particularly for German offshore capacity. We evaluated the issue
by proposing an algorithm to refit the capacity.
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
77
==PAGE:88==
6.2
Future Work
This project was limited by a time deadline. If we have had more time available it would
be interesting to do following:
• Create a state­of­art deep neural network such as long short­term memory (LSTM),
that is able to capture order dependence in sequence prediction problems and eval­
uate its predictive performance. As well explore techniques for improving the desrip­
tive accuracy of neural network using post­hoc agnostics methods.
• Create a dashboard where we could see all models and all targets. In this work we
have only analyzed the desriptive accuracy of the target: UK’s offshore wind load
factor. Even though the results generalize pretty well to the other targets it would
benificial to see the other targets in action. As well as give the stakeholders an
option to play around on their own.
• Improve the feature enginnering. In this work we have worked with raw data and
the wind power curve transformation. It would perhaps be worth to include temperal
feautures that potentially could improve the predictive accuracy.
• Perform Shapley value regression to propperly understand contribution of weight/­
coefficient to the target variable in linear regression model. The works of [PA16]
look like a promising direction.
• Evaluate the predictive performance for different levels of wind speeds to uncover
potentially issues with models.
In this work we have only evalauted the mean
squared error. The mean might hide some areas where we could imporve the model
if we had analyzed the performance in different part of feature space.
• Utlize the LIME framework. In beginning of this thesis we quickly deemed the post
hoc model­agnostic method SHAP to be the most powerfull of the two (SHAP and
LIME) given it robust background in game theory. The largest advantages of LIME
is propperly it is computaionally faster. However, it could be interesting what LIME
could offer to improve the descriptive accurarcy.
• Use a scheme that performs automatic feature selection for the GAMs. The de­
scriptive accuracy of GAMs is a product of the sparsity in the number of component
functions and smoothness of the component functions. We have seen in this work
how we can perform feature selection by fitting a lasso model with power curved data
and how the smoothness makes the curve look more like a power curve. However,
our method to empose sparsity can potentially fail to identify valuable non­linear
relationships that do not follow the specific power curved pattern. It would be ad­
vantageous to make the GAM itself perform feature selection. The works of [HSS19]
could potentially be a place to start this work.
• Analyze the data associated with Germany and the United Kingdom’s offshore wind
load factor. For these two targets, we identified significant misalignment with the
governmental statistics. Furthermore, the models created tend to perform worse for
these two targets. It would be worth investigating the underlying data more ­ notably
as these two targets are important markets for Ørsted.
• Improve the cross­validation scheme such taht utlize all the available data. In this
work, we have used 01/01/2019­31/12/2019 as a test set. If we added an additional
5­fold layer to the cross­validation scheme, such that it used all five years as a hold­
out test set, we could have estimated the generalization error better.
78
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:89==
• Consider more features at each node. In this work, we only used a single feature per
node (we got multiple features by considering multiple nodes). While this is efficient
to limit ourselves and enforce sparsity, it might harm the predictive accuracy. It would
be worth investigating if more features at each node would improve the predictive
performance and how it would affect the descriptive accuracy.

A
Modeling & Model Agnostic Methods
Theory
This appendix chapter will describe the underlying theory for the models and post­hoc
model agnostic methods we used in chapter 4 and chapter 5. The chapter is composed
of four sections described below.
1. Linear Modeling. In the first section, we will present the theory of linear models.
We describe how adding constraints to the linear models can ensure that they live
up to the Analysts’ constraints and how we with regularization can introduce sparsity
to the models. Furthermore, we present how we can create a mean benchmark and
multivariate benchmark using linear models.
2. XGBoost. In the second section, we present the theory behind gradient boosting
model XGBoost.
3. GAM. Finally, in the third section, we describe the theory behind GAMS and how we
fit them and regularize them.
4. SHAP. Finally in the fouth section we describe the model­agnostic method SHAP
and the underlying Shapley value.
A.1
Linear Modeling
In this section, we present the theory for linear modeling. We will go through the classical
linear model, the constraints necessary to meet the Analysts’ requirements, and how we
can impose regularization that ensures sparsity. Furthermore, we will present the theory
behind a mean benchmark model and multivariate linear benchmark model. Finally, we
will learn about what software/libraries we used to implement the various linear models.
The works of [HTF09], [cona], and [conb] is the inspiration for the theory that we will
present in this section.
A.1.1
Linear Regression Model
A linear regression model predicts the target as a weighted sum of the feature inputs. Lin­
ear models are used to model the dependence of a regression target y on some features
x has the form
y = β0 +
p

xjβj + ε.
(A.1)
where ε ∼N(0, σ) and the predicted outcome of an instance is a weighted sum of its p fea­
tures. If Equation A.1 has more than p > 1 inputs is called the multiple linear regression
model. The betas (βj) serve as the learned feature weights. The first beta (β0) is the inter­
cept of the model. We usually use the ordinary least squares method to find the weights
that minimize the squared differences between the actual and the estimated outcomes.
The method minimize the squared differences between the target and the predictions:
ˆβ = arg min
β0,...,βp

Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
83
==PAGE:94==
While a unique closed­form solution that minimizes the squared differences between the
targets and predictions exist1, we will only present the optimization scheme. It is more
in line with the extensions to the linear model we will present in the upcoming sub­sub­
sections.
The linearity of the model makes the estimation procedure simple (the weights make the
model modular). We can estimate the weight(s) with confidence intervals. However, we
can only trust weights to be ”correctly” estimated if the data meets certain assumptions.
In total, five assumptions have to hold. We have described the five in the list below.
1. Linear relationship: There must be a linear relationship between the target and
dependent variables. A linear relationship suggests that a change in response y
due to one unit change in xj is constant. If we fit a linear model to a non­linear,
non­additive data set, the regression algorithm would fail to capture the trend math­
ematically, resulting in an inefficient model.
2. Normality: The residuals of the regression should follow a normal distribution. If
the residuals do not follow a normal distribution, the estimated confidence intervals
may become too wide or narrow.
3. No multicollinearity: The correlation between the independent variables is low
or none. With the presence of multicollinearity, it becomes difficult to estimate the
weight in linear regression. This because the feature effects are additive, and it
becomes indeterminable to which of the correlated features to attribute the effects.
In other words, it becomes problematic to figure out which variable is contributing to
the prediction.
4. No autocorrelation: The residuals/errors contain little or not no autocorrelation, i.e.,
the residuals are independent of one another. The presence of autocorrelation in
the error terms make the model underestimate the actual standard error.
5. Homoscedasticity: The variance should be constant over the entire feature space.
The presence of non­constant variance in the error terms viloates this. Generally,
non­constant variance arises in the presence of outliers. These observations get
too much influence, thereby disproportionately affects the model’s performance.
If we violate these assumptions, we can still achieve great predictive accuracy; however,
we lose descriptive accuracy because the weights might not be estimated. In other words,
there is no guaranty for meaningfull estimated weight/coefficient. We can still use the
modularity of the estimated weight to make an accurate prediction.
A.1.2
Linear Regression with the Analysts Constraints
We recall that the Analysts have the following five constraints to the models:
C1 The model has to be linear.
C2 Specific power curve transformations have to be used when modeling the wind load
factors.
C3 The sum of the weights associated with each feature has to sum to one.
C4 The convex combination of features needs to make physical sense.
C5 The choice of nodes in the model has to be native to what it is predicting.
1A unique closed­form solution only exists when the number of observations is greater than the number
of parameters, i.e., p < n. If p > n, there is no longer a unique least squares coefficient estimate.

(C2), and (C5). To fulfill the two remaining constraints, (C3) and (C4), we can impose the
following two modelling­constraints to the optimizaiton problem:

With lasso regresion (least absolute shrinkage and selection operator) we seek to min­

When there are many correlated variables in a linear regression model, their coefficients
can become poorly determined and exhibit high variance. By imposing a size constraint
on the coefficient such as in Equation A.9 we elevate the problem with high variance. How­
ever, the coefficients can still become poorly determined from an interpretability point of
view. The weight associated with each feature does still not ensure to represent the rela­
tive feature importance properly. When we impose the size constraints λ in Equation A.9
it shrinks the magnitude of the coefficient of the model and less important features, or
the highly correlated ”cousin” of an important feature shrinks all the way to zero. In other
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
85
==PAGE:96==
words, the size constraint acts as an automatic way to introduce sparsity into the multiple
linear regression model. When we impose regularization such as the lasso, we need to
standardize the data because the solutions are not equivalent to the inputs’ scaling.
The term |βj| is the called the L1­norm. It ensures that many weights receive an estimate
of 0 and shrinks others. We can see the penalization term λ as a tuning parameter. We
can find λ to minimize the model error with cross­validation or use λ as a parameter to
control the interpretability of the model. The larger the penalization, the fewer features
are present in the model (because their weights are zero), and the better the model can
be interpreted.
We can also impose the following constraint
0 ≤βi
for
i = 1, ..., p
(A.10)
to the optimizaiton problem such that we force the coefficient positive.
Computing the lasso solution is a quadratic programming problem. However, efficient
algorithms are available for computing the entire path of solutions as λ is varied. An
example of such an algorithm is the Least Angle Regression algorithm.
If we are interested in enforcing more sparsity than the optimal cross­validation error, we
can consider the ”ℓ0­norm”. The ℓ0­norm is not a norm, but a scheme made up to ensure
more sparsity without sacrificing too much predictive accuracy. We can enforce it by
considering
ˆβ = argmin
β

R2(β) −µ Number of features used (β)
total number of features

(A.11)
Where R2 is the coefficient of determiniantion i.e.
R2 = 1 −
PN
i=1 (yi −¯y)2
PN
i=1 (yi −byi)2 ,
(A.12)
and µ is a non­negative hyper­parameter. When µ = 0, we select the level of regulariza­
tion that yields the lowest cross­validation error. When µ > 0, we impose more regulariza­
tion. In practice, a choice of either µ = 0.1 or µ = 0.2 turned out to increase regularization.
It yielded a small number of features while the predictive accuracy remained close to the
solution with the smallest cross­validation error. The authors of [Lev+17] proposed a sim­
ilar scheme where the authors enforced more sparsity into a classification problem by
imposing a similar constraint. Instead of considering the R2, they considered the AUC
for classification. We found inspiration for the L0­norm for regression by considering the
authors’ ”L0­norm” for clarification.
A.1.6
Implemtation
The linear regression, the mean model, and the lasso model is implemented in the scikit
learn [Ped+11] python library. We have implemented the Analysts preferred model and
the multivariate benchmark model on top of the scikit learn framework.
A.2
XGBoost
XGBoost (eXtreme Gradient Boosting) is an algorithm that has recently been dominating
applied machine learning, and Kaggle2 competitions for structured or tabular data [Bro16].
2Kaggle is an online platform for data scientists. Kaggle allows its users to create and participate in data
science competitions. For more information see www.kaggle.com.
86
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:97==
Essentially XGBoost is just a gradient boosting model. The extreme refers to the engi­
neering goal to push the limit of computations resources for boosted tree algorithms to
the limit. In other words, we can think of XGBoost as a hyper­optimized version of a gra­
dient boosting model. Gradient boosting refers to a class of ensemble machine learning
algorithms we can use for classification or regression predictive modeling problems. We
construct ensembles from decision tree models. Trees are added one at a time to the
ensemble and fit to correct the prediction errors made by prior trees. This is known as
boosting.
A.2.1
Boosted Trees
With boosting, we build trees sequentially. That means that the subsequent tree’s purpose
is to reduce the error of the previous tree. In other words, every tree learns from its pre­
decessors and updates the residual errors. In boosting, we use weak learners, i.e., small
decision trees that exhibit high bias with a predictive power just a bit better than random
guessing. Each weak learner contributes with important information for the prediction and
thereby transforming weak learners into strong learners. The gradient boosting algorithm
is also known for ”garnering wisdom from a council of fools”.
The question remains; how do we find the weak learner in the first place? We have based
the upcoming derivation from the works of [Che16].
Let the objective function be defined as follows

A.2.2
Implementation
We have utilized the popular open­source library xgboost by [Che16]. The library probably
provides the most efficient and effective implementation of the gradient boosting algorithm.
The library has as Python API we have utilized.
A.2.3
XGBoost Hyperparameters
XGboost comes with many hyperparameters. We will go through what we believe are the
five most important.
1. Number of Trees [n_estimators. default set to 100.] The parameter controls the
number of trees the ensemble algorithm will utilize.
2. Tree Depth [max_depth default set to 6]. The depth of trees controls how weak
the learners we utilize are. With a too shallow tree, we end with learners that do
not bring new information. On the other hand, with too strong learners, we end up
overfitting.
3For more information about regularization and xgboost in general, we encourage to visit https://xgboost.
readthedocs.io/en/latest/tutorials/model.html.
88
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:99==
3. Learning Rate [eta default set to 0.3]. The learning rate controls how much a new
tree contributes to ensemble prediction. With a low rate, we often require more trees,
but we benefit from more learners.
4. Number of Samples [subsample default set to 1.0 (the entire training dataset)]. The
number denotes how much of the training set we use for training the tree. With fewer
samples, we introduce more variance in each tree (but it can benefit the model.)
5. Number of Features [colsample_bytree default set to 1.0 (100 percent of the fea­
tures)]. We can control the number of features we use to fit each decision tree
with this hyperparameter. We introduce additional variance when we utilize fewer
features (but like the hyperparameter number of samples that can also improve per­
formance).
For more information about all hyperparameter, we refer to https://xgboost.readthedocs.io/
en/latest/parameter.html.
Hyper parameters for the case. Unfortunately, performing an xgboost hyperparameter
search for every model is infeasible. So instead, we performed a hyperparameter search
on a small subset and found that the following parameter ensures great generalization
(but they can not be guaranteed to be optimal.): n_estimators = 100, max_depth = 5,
eta = 0.100333, subsample = 0.5, and colsample_bytree = 0.8
A.3
GAM
GAM (generalized additive models) is an automatic flexible statisical method that can
model nonlinear effects. The works of [HTF09], [cona], and [conb] is the inspiration for
the theory that we will present in this section The GAM has the following form
g(E[y]) = β0 +
p
X
j=1
fj (xj) + ε.
(A.27)
Where xj indicates the jth feature, g(·) is a link function and the fj’s are unspecified
smooth function, potentially nonlinear. GAMs are a flexible extension of generalized linear
models framework (GLMs). That means depending on the response data; we can select
the appropriate link function.
We can plot each component function with fj(xj) as a function of xj to see the contribution
of a single feature to the prediction. Particularly when the number of component functions
fj(·) and features xj, the model allows for great global and local interpretation.
A.3.1
Fitting GAMs
There are many different ways to fit GAMs. The traditional way is to use back fitting, where
we iteratively train a component function to best fit the residuals from the other.

special cases are:
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
89
==PAGE:100==
1. λ →0: no smoothing), the smoothing spline converges to the interpolating spline.
2. λ →∞: (infinite smoothing), the roughness penalty becomes paramount, and the
estimate converges to a linear least­squares estimate.
We find the fixed smoothing parameter λ with (generalized) cross­validation.

A.3.2

Recall we are trying to predict the fraction load factor =

from Gaussian distribution. However, we will still use the identity link even though we are
not guaranteed to predict sensical prediction, i.e., less than zero or greater than one.

cumulative distribution function). However, we assume the transformation yield less inter­
pretable features. E.g. if consider the use of logit link function i.e.

A.3.3
Implemtation
We have implemented our GAMs with the Python package Pygam [Dan18]. The package
is by all means still in its early stages, and there are many known issues with the pack­
age. To compensate for this, we have used the building blocks from the Pygam package
to create our ”own” GAM model that worked with the scikit­learn library. The PyGAM
package estimates λ uses generalized cross validation, but with our ”scikit­learn” imple­
mentation, we can use normal cross­validation. We have implemented this such that the
cross­validation error better could be compared with the cross validation error of the other
models.
A.4
SHAP
SHAP (SHapley Additive Explanations) is post­hoc model agonistic method that can help
us achieve higher local interpretability. Shapley values form the foundation for SHAP.
Shapley values are a solution concept in cooperative game theory.
This section is composed of five subsections:
1. Definition of The Shapley Values and Their Additive Explanatory Power. In the
first subsection, we will explain the Shapley value and how we can use it to compute
local additive feature contributions.
2. Approximations Algorithms. In the second subsections, we present two com­
monly used algorithms that allow for fast computation of approximate SHAP values.
3. Common SHAP Explanations Plot In the third subsection, we introduce several
popular visualizations to explain models using SHAP values.
4. Implementation. Finally, in the last subsections, we talk about the Python package
we used to utilize SHAP.
The works of [Mol19] is the primary source of inspiration for the theory that we will present
in this section.
A.4.1
Definition of The Shapley Values and Their Additive Explanatory
Power
The name of Shapley originates from its Author, the American Mathematician and Nobel
Prize­winning economist Lloyd Shapley. Lloyd Shapley’s idea is to consider a cooperative
game where players form a collision and work together to obtain a price/payout. According
to Lloyd Shapley’s idea, each player should receive a payout/reward proportional to their
marginal contributions in the game. The Shapley value is assigned accordingly to each
player’s ”effort” in the game. Thus, we should assign a Shapley value to players according
to their influence on the price/payout.
Regarding interpretable machine learning, we can view the game as our model, the fea­
tures as players, and the model’s prediction as to the game’s payout. The Shapley values
tell us how to distribute the ”payout” among the features fairly. [Mol19] describes the fol­
lowing intuitive way to understand how we calculate Shapley Values for a single prediction:
”The feature values enter a room in random order. All feature values in the room partici­
pate in the game (= contribute to the prediction). The Shapley value of a feature value is
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
91
==PAGE:102==
the average change in the prediction that the coalition already in the room receives when
the feature value joins them.”
We can formlize the above by letting
• S be a subset of all feature sets,
• valx(S) be the value function. I.e., it is the payout function for coalitions of play­
ers S (feature values). valx(S) is the prediction for feature values in set S that we
marginalize over features that we do not include in set S,
• x is the vector of feature values of the instance to be explained and
• p the number of features.
The Shapley value allocated to feature j is equal to the average marginal contribution of
feature j overall possible orderings:
ϕj(val) = 1
p!
X
S⊆{x1,...,xp}\{xj}
|S|!(p −|S| −1)! (val (S ∪{xj}) −val(S))
(A.33)
Properties of Shapley Values
The Shapley value is the only attribution method that satisfies the properties Efficiency,
Symmetry, Dummy and Additivity, which together can be considered a definition of a fair
payout [Sha53]. The value is fair because it is the only distribution that satisfies the four
desirable properties listed below.
1. Efficiency. The efficiency property distributes the predictions from the model amoung
the features such that they add to the difference of prediction for x and average.
p
X
j=1
ϕj = ˆf(x) −EX( ˆf(X))
(A.34)
2. Symmetry. The symmetry property requires that if two feature values j and k have
a symmetrical contribution, they contribute equally.
∀S ∈M\{j, k} : v(S ∪{j}) = v(S ∪{k}) ⇒ϕj(v) = ϕk(v)
(A.35)
3. Dummy. If a feature does not change the prediction, that feature should have a
Shapley value of 0.
∀S ∈M\{j} : v(S ∪{j}) = v(S) ⇒ϕj(v) = 0
(A.36)
4. Additivity. The additivity axiom states that if we re­model the setting as a single
game in which each coalition (set of feature) S achieves a payoff of ϕj(v) + ϕj(w),
the features’ payoff in each coalition should be the sum of the payoff they would
have achieved for that coalition under the two separate models
∀j ∈M : ϕj(v + w) = ϕj(v) + ϕj(w)
(A.37)
We can explain the intuition of the Additivity property better by considering the follow­
ing example. If we consider a prediction from a random forest model, the predicted
value is the average of many decision trees. If we calculate the Shapley value for
each tree individually, average them, we get the same Shapley value as had we
considered the Shapley value for the entire random forest.
92
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:103==
Shapley Additive Explanations
For each feature and each prediction, we can calculate a Shapley Value. The features’
associated Shapley value forms an additive explanation (SHAP) for that prediction. The
contribution of a feature value to the final prediction is known as the SHAP value. If
we consider a model M and let n be the number of observations and p the number of
features we can consider the associated feature space xij where i = 1, ..., n, and j =
1, ..., p. SHAP explain how a feature value j = 1, ..., p of observation i contributes the
predictive difference between the model expected values E [M] and the prediction for
instance M(xi).
The Shapley Value associated with feature j for observation i is how much the feature
affect the prediction in the unit of the prediction. That means we decompose the models
prediction as follows:
M(xi) = E [M] +
p
X
j=1
ϕj = ϕ0 +
p
X
j=1
ϕj = ˆyi
(A.38)
Note how Equation A.38 resembles the linear model. While the comparison is not one­to­
one to the linear model, the analogy is close. The expression ϕ0 represents the expected
value of the model (not the intercept in linear regression). The remaining coefficients
ϕj represent how each feature i = 1, ..., p affects the expected value of the model. In
other words, for linear models, we can infer the Shapley values by reading the partial
dependence plot (PDP). Recall we described the PDP in subsection 2.5.2. SHAP values
of all the input features will always sum up the difference between baseline (expected)
model output and the current model output for the prediction we explain. Hence we can
use SHAP values to infer the feature importance of the linear model. This would not
be possible by just examining the weight of the linear model because the value of each
coefficient depends on the scale of the input features.
As SHAP at its core is Shapley values, we get all the advantages of Shapley values,
namely the solid theoretical foundation in game theory and we know the prediction is
distributed fairly among the feature values. The axioms: efficiency, symmetry, dummy,
additivity give the SHAP feature explanation a robust foundation.
While SHAP has excellent properties, it turns out calculating them for ML models predic­
tions requires a lot of computing time. From Equation A.33 we can see we need to iterate
over all possible permutations of features 4. In other words, the computation time grows
exponentially and becomes intractable for more than, say, ten features [LL17].
A.4.2
Approximations Algorithms
To deal with the computational complexity of calculating SHAP, researchers have pro­
posed approximation algorithms to compensate. We will briefly present two popular al­
gorithms, namely the Kernel SHAP and the Tree­SHAP algorithm. The first is a model­
agnostic approximation algorithm, and the latter a model­specific algorithm.
Kernel SHAP
Kernal SHAP is a model­agonistic algorithm proposed by [LL17] that approximates Shap­
ley values of each feature for a prediction. Briefly described the Kernal SHAP algorithm
works by sample feature subsets. For each feature subset, the algorithm fits a linear
4Most ML models design do not allow for empty features. To compensate, we let the feature that does not
participate in the ”game” be their expected value in the feature space.
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
93
==PAGE:104==
regression model. The algorithm assumes independent features. The average weights/­
coefficients of the linear regressions
Y = x1β1 + x2β2 + ... + xpβp
(A.39)
can be interpreted as approximated Shapley Values. With the linear regression, we can
approximate all Shapley values simultaneously, which is much faster than calculating the
precise Shapley value. While the method is much faster than calculating the precise
Shapley values, the method is still relatively slow for practical purposes.
Tree SHAP
The Tree SHAP is an algorithm that provided exact Shapley values for tree­based models
such as Decision Tree, Random Forrest, and XGBoost. As the algorithm is both very
complicated and does not bring much additional theory to how we can utilize SHAP for
interpretability, we will not explain it. For details on the algorithm, we refer to [LEL18]. To
paint a course picture of the algorithm, we can think of it as levering the tree structure
of the models to compute the conditional expectations. This is done recursively at the
same time as the algorithm audit the proportion of possible feature subsets. That way,
the algorithm can efficiently find the weight contribution of each subset.
Tree SHAP allows us to compute exact SHAP feature contribution in polynomial time.
Compared to exact KernelSHAP, it reduces the computational complexity from O(TL2P )
to O(TLD2), where T is the number of trees, L is the maximum number of leaves in any
tree, P the number of features, and D the maximal depth of any tree [Mol19].
A.4.3
Common SHAP Explanations Plot
SHAP comes with many global interpretation methods based on aggregations of Shapley
values. In this subsection, we will present the following three popular SHAP Explanations
Plot:
1. SHAP Feature Importance Plot
2. SHAP Dependence Plot
3. SHAP Waterfall Plot
We will motivate the explaination plots with a toy­example. In the toy­example we will use
xgboosting regressor to prediction the median value of owner occupied home in $ 1000s
for the popular Boston Housing Dataset. The prediction is based properties of house and
socio­ecomocis data of the area around the house. The Boston data consist of 506 rows
and 14 columns where the first 13 are the features and the last (medv) is the target. The
14 columns are briefly describe in the list below:
1. crim: per capita crime rate by town.
2. zn: proportion of residential land zoned for lots over 25,000 sq.ft.
3. indus: proportion of non­retail business acres per town.
4. chas: Charles River dummy variable (= 1 if tract bounds river; 0 otherwise).
5. nox: nitrogen oxides concentration (parts per 10 million).
6. rm: average number of rooms per dwelling.
7. age: proportion of owner­occupied units built prior to 1940.
8. dis: weighted mean of distances to five Boston employment centres.
94
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:105==
9. rad: index of accessibility to radial highways.
10. tax: full­value property­tax rate per $10,000.
11. ptratio: pupil­teacher ratio by town.
12. black: 1000(Bk −0.63)2 where Bk is the proportion of blacks by town.
13. lstat: lower status of the population (percent).
14. medv: median value of owner­occupied homes in $1000s.
Let explore how we can reveal what the xgboost regressor has learned about the data
using SHAP values. First, recall that we can find the SHAP values quickly with the Tree­
SHAP algorithm.

across the data:

Figure A.2: SHAP dependence plot for feature lstat (lower status of the population (per­

SHAP Watefall Plot
We use Waterfall plots to show explanations for individual predictions (local interpretabil­
ity). So we provide a single prediction’s associated Shapley values as input. The lower
part of the waterfall plot begins with the expected value of the model output. Each column
afterward shows how each feature’s positively (red) or negatively (blue) contributes to the
prediction.
In Figure A.3 have we plotted a waterfall plot for a specific prediction by xgboost regressor.
The figure shows how the model arrived at the prediction of a cost of 24.019 [×$1000]. The
baseline the average predicted house price is 22.234 [×$1000]. Among others, we see
the price is affected positively by the feature lstat value but negatively for DIS and CRIM.
This plot can help justify action based on model output (O4) especially combined with
SHAP dependence plots.
A.4.4
Implementation
The approximation algorithm and the SHAP explanations plots we presented are all part

C
Description of Govermental Data
C.1
France
As an external source for France’s aggregated yearly generation and development of
capacity in renewables, we will consult British Petroleum’s ’Statistical Review of World
Energy – all data, 1965­2019’ [Bri20]. Initially, we searched for an ”official” governmental
source in the French Ministry of Ecology Sustainable Development and Energy (Ministère
de l’Écologie) but without luck. British Petroleum publishes the yearly generation and
cumulative capacity yearly but for combined wind production, i.e., offshore and onshore
wind. However, before 2020 France has not had any offshore wind generation, so the
problem is elevated. The offshore wind generation is still listed but with zero production
to follow the format of Germany and the United Kingdom.

United Kingdom
As an external source for aggregated yearly generation and development of capacity in

As the cumulative capacity is listed in the raw data [US20] the capacity of the last quarter
is used to describe the capacity of the year i.e.
Yearly Capacity = Capacity Q4.

D
The capacity adjusting algorithm
We have built the algorithm for refitting the capacity on following two assumptions:
1. The cumulative capacity never decreases: From the provided capacity data, we see
the pattern that three countries install a greater share of renewable is than they retire
every year. We expect this trend to continue as the countries need to expand the
renewable capacity to reach the climate goals. However, in theory, there could be
years where a greater share of, e.g., wind turbines reach the end of their service life
than new ones installed. The algorithm does not count for that.
2. The capacity is always greater or equal to the generation: The second assump­
tion is the nature of what capacity means; we can at maximum generate energy
corrosponding to the capacity level
The algorithm consists of four steps. We explain each of the four steps in the context of
the example of refitting Germany’s offshore capacity.
D.1
Step 1. Extend the generation period with µ days
In the first step of the algorithm, we extend the period of available generation data by
copying the first µ days of generation and the last µ days of data and add it to the respect­
ing ends. Figure D.1 illustrate the first step in the algorithm. We can see that we have
extended the period of generation data with µ = 45 days from the figure. The extensions
are copies of the first and last part of the period of generation data. In practice, µ = 45
(a month and a half) worked well for offshore and onshore wind. That way, the exten­
sion captured some period with high winds. For refitting the capacity of solar, we choose
µ = 185 (half a year). That way, we captured half the summer, a period with lot of sun.
D.2
Step 2. Fit and correct 30­day rolling max window
In the second step, we fit a rolling max window to the extended generation period. We
based the rolling max window on 30 days, as it in practice worked well and resembled a
month. But in theory it is hyperparameter that could be tuned. As the fitted rolling moving
max window at times decreases, against the assumptions, we have to correct this. The
idea is to replace all values in the window that are lower than the current highest value
with the current highest value. We will initialize the current highest value as the first value
of the rolling window, and we will update it when we encounter a higher value. The above
scheme is formulated in Algorithm 2. We can see the fitted rolling max window and the
corrected version in Figure D.2, marked with black and red, respectively.
114
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:125==
Algorithm 2 Correct Rolling Moving Max Window
1: function Correct Window(W)
2:
H ←W[1]
3:
N ←length(W)
4:
for i ←1 to N do
5:
if W[i] > H then
6:
H ←W[i]
7:
else
8:
W[i] ←H
9:
end if
10:
end for
11:
return W
12: end function

D.3
Step 3. Smooth the corrected 30­day rolling max window
It turned out that a smooth corrected rolling max window worked better in practice. We
achieved this by taking a 30­day rolling mean window of the corrected 30­day rolling max
window. In Figure D.3 can we see the smooth corrected rolling max window with gener­
ation data in the original period (01/01/2015 ­ 31/12/2019). Note here that the smooth
window violates the second assumption of the algorithm, namely that the generation can
never be greater than the capacity. We will fix this in the next step of the algorithm.
D.4
Step 4. Fit smoothed corrected window to the
governmental capacity
The smooth corrected rolling max window, resembling the capacity, underestimates the
actual capacity, and the generation is at times greater than the capacity. We will correct
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
115
==PAGE:126==
this by multiplying the rolling max window with constant κ. We estimated κ by comparing
the aggregated yearly load factors based on the smooth­rolling max window as the capac­
ity to governmental statistics. We can see difference in Figure D.4. It is clear that we now
overestimate the yearly mean load factor. The idea is to find the constant κ such that the
calculated load factor using the fitted capacity is in line with the governmental statistics.
We achieved this by letting
• GCy denote cumulative governmental capacity at year y, and
• FCi,y denote the fitted capacity (the smooth­rolling max window) at hour h in year y.
We can estimate κ minimizing the euclidian distance between the two yearly aggregated
load factors:
κ = argmin

In the context of the example with german offshore wind generation, we estimate κ =
1.156. In Figure D.6, the calculated aggregated yearly mean load factor is much more in
line with the governmental statistics. In Figure D.5 we can see the difference between
smooth rolling max window marked with red and the corresponding fit multiplied with κ =
1.156 marked with a dashed blue line.

Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
131
==PAGE:142==
G
Project Plan
G.1
Context and Motivaton
This thesis is centered around solving a case study based on a recent project of the Ørsted
Analytics Team. The case aimed at inferring a country’s past relative solar and wind
power production from historical node meteorological forecasts. The resulting historical
yearly wind and solar generation profiles are used as scenarios for Ørsted’s electricity
price forecasting tool. Hence, they determine the uncertainty space of the electricity price
and the risk attitude of any decision taken based upon it.
At Ørsted, traders generate a bulk of the company’s profits making decisions based on
electricity price forecasts made from the forecasting tool. Many of these forecasts are
based on simple statistical analyses and do not exploit recent developments in machine
learning. Thus, predictive models could substantially improve the accuracy of those fore­
casts and ultimately, lead to better and more profitable trading decisions. However, know­
ing why a prediction is made is crucial, as millions of DKK are potentially at risk when using
incomprehensible model outputs to support and drive trading decisions.
Machine learning models have the potential to automate and substantially improve the
outcome of processes that have traditionally been executed by human operators. How­
ever, the lack of transparency and accountability of predictive models represents a major
barrier to their adoption in practice.
In summary, the thesis will explore two parts. Namely solving the case study of inferring
a country’s past relative solar and wind power production using machine learning. This
will be denoted The Analytics Problem. The second part is to investigate the trade­off
between interpretability and accuracy of machine learning models and investigate meth­
ods to alleviate it. This will be denoted The Interpretability Problem. The two parts are
eloborated further in subsection G.1.1 and subsection G.1.2.
G.1.1
The Analytics Problem
The analytics problem is a case study based on a recent project of the Ørsted Analytics
Team, which aimed at inferring a country’s past relative solar and wind power production
from historical node meteorological forecasts. The relative renewable energy production
is the ratio between the capacity and what is generated. This ratio is denoted the load
factor and inferring this measure is denoted load factor problem. The load factor problem
will be solved using supervised machine learning where models based on periods where
the load factor is already estimated will be created to infer the periods where only historical
node meteorological forecasts are available. The data is elaborated further in section G.2.
G.1.2
The Interpretability Problem
The term interpretability is ill­defined in the field of machine learning [Lip16]. And it not
clear how to measure interpretability [Mol19]. This has to be defined in more clear terms
before any Machine learning modeling. Leaving that an open problem for now. However,
the intial idea of accessing this is described in section G.4.
G.2
Data
Data available for wind load factors (hourly resolution and for both offshore and onshore):
1. Node wind speed forecasts from a vendor (from 2000 to 2020)
132
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
==PAGE:143==
2. Measured wind power production per country from start 2015 to end 2019
3. Installed wind power capacity per country from start 2015 to end 2019
Data available for solar load factors (hourly resolution):
1. Node solar load factors for adjustable solar panels from a vendor (from 2000 to
2020)
2. Node solar load factors for fixed solar panels from a vendor (from 2000 to 2020)
3. Measured solar power production per country from start 2015 to end 2019
4. Installed solar power capacity per country from start 2015 to end 2019
The aim is to develop a predictive model so that a country load factor for all of the past
20 years. That is build a model based on the periods start 2015 to end 2019 and use the
model to label the data from 2000­2015. Such that all 20 years can be projected onto
future years for scenario analyses (e.g., “if country X had similar wind conditions in 2021
as in 2002, what would that look like.
These four countries of interest namely Germany (GE), France (FR), The Netherlands
(NL), and The United Kingdom (UK). The node data come from 54 strategic points onshore
and offshore in these countries. The list below summarizes the number of nodes there is
in each country:
• UK: 21
• GE: 17
• FR: 12
• NL: 4
G.3
Learning Objectives
A student who has met the objectives of this project will be able to:
• Define interpretability specific cases such as the Ørsted Analytics Team case.
• Work with big data in the programming language Python.
• Design and apply machine learning methods for solving the regression problem in
the programming language Python.
• Evaluate model performance both in terms of accuracy and interpretability.
• Examine Model­Agnostic Methods to explain complex models.
G.4
Methodology
The first step of the project will consist on exploring and preprocessing the dataset. Once
the dataset is assumed ready feature related to solar and wind power generation will be
created. Interpretability will be sought defined prior to the modeling as well as measures
on the evaluation of interpretability. Afterward interpretable models like linear regression
and decision trees will be applied to solve the load factor problem. Gradually, more com­
plexity will be added and the impact on performance and interpretability will be examined.
As the models become more complex Model­Agnostic Methods methods will be examined
to explain the complex models. Here methods such as the following will be explored:
1. Partial Dependence Plot (PDP)
Interpretable Machine Learning as Decision Support for Analysts and Traders
133
==PAGE:144==
2. Individual Conditional Expectation (ICE)
3. Feature Interaction Methods
4. Local Surrogate (LIME)
5. SHAP (SHapley Additive exPlanations)
If time permits, a state­of­art black­box model, like a deep neural network, will be created.
G.5
Planning and scheduling

chart below.
